### Post by erkki70 on 2005-03-31
Hi,

Ubuntu PPC works really fine on most Macs. There are though issues about having a decent Flash player and plugin, wifi with Airport cards, running Skype, sound on Mac mini, etc...
The overall is _very very good_ but according to what the user wants, needs and how much he's ready to dive into command line, there are definitely restrictions and yet unsolved issues.

As you can anyway install Ubuntu along Mac OS X, and have dual boot through yaboot (or BootX on older macs), it is a very good way to get into it.
Live CD allow you to discover Ubuntu without even installing it, system runs from CD.

Mac mini install and issues are very well documented in the forum and you'll find a bunch of useful info when typing &quot;mac mini&quot; in the search tool. ;)

As it looks like you're already experienced on i386 platform, getting the best of Ubuntu PPC shouldn't be too difficult when installing on Mac mini ;)

---

### Post by tater on 2005-03-31
It all depends on why your mother-in-law insists on a Mac.  If it is because she is familiar with the MacOS and wants to stick with it, then your putting Ubuntu on her Mac will be just as unacceptable to her as switching to, say, a Windows XP machine.  Yes?

I went to Ubuntu because my old Powerbook Pismo was running MacOS9, which did not let me access shared folders on my Window XP machines, nor use my networked printer.  I knew that OSX would provide this functionality, but at the same time, I figured OSX would run slower and take up more hard drive space on this Powerbook than a linux-distro would.

I chose Ubuntu because it seems to be the most highly supported, with the least hassle, distro for Macs.

Many things worked after install.  But fine-tuning took several days of tweaking.  A couple that come to mind are figuring out how to tune my video driver, and how to play DVDs without choppiness.

And now I have spent over a week of long hours and total frustration trying to get printing to work, only to have finally confirmed that my printer is not supported (no powerpc linux printer driver that will work on my printer, period).  This is a biggie, so if you intend to print, buy a printer that is supported in ppc linux (my printer has a linux driver, but it is for i386 only, not for macs.  And the company told me it will never make mac linux drivers for their printers).

Hope this helps,
tater

### Post by synd on 2005-04-01
I totally agree. If its a new mac (ie. that isnt allowed to boot into OS9) then keep OSX on there. If you're getting an older Mac such as an iBook clamshell or a iMac DV, then replacing OS9 (rather than upping to OSX) is a better idea IMHO.

OSX is stable and very much like linux. Except it's optimized to work with Macs. If you want to dual-boot and let your mother decide, then I recommend that.

Theres just no way I could imagine an iMac G5 running Ubuntu instead of OSX..

### Post by erkki70 on 2005-04-03
[QUOTE=teumima]your saying no flash player on MAc, because you can get flash player to work on firefox in linux.
Need to download the flashplayer and install it to your firefox directory.[/QUOTE]
Unfortunately it doesn't quite work this way on PPC specific arch.
Search the forums eg, [http://ubuntuforums.org/search.php?searchid=452711](http://ubuntuforums.org/search.php?searchid=452711)
What you'll get is a flash player able to read "old" flash anims. (maybe up to version 5 so quite dated).
Anything MX or version 7 for example just doesn't appear, you cannot see the anim. You can access the sites with flash content though after installing the player and plugin mentionned in the posts.
Better than nothing but nothing to do with what you got on a PC.

PPC is a different architecture and there are more issues as mentionned previously.

So if you're mother-in-law is a flash addict, better to stick with Mac OS  ;-) (only joking but, read several posts in the Ubuntu PPC users section and you'll get the point...)

### Post by philcolbourn on 2005-04-10
macs and installing linux on them was new to me. I went for a dual boot, and I am still working out what is best for me.

I have a new powerbook g4 (2005 version) and the 80211g card is not supported (not many are on any platform I think). The touchpad also does not work. I need to get a usb (or perhaps bluetooth) mouse to be able to use the thing.

Osx is good, but a little more buggy than I had expected. eg. the disk utility crashes, will not copy an iso and will not burn a ubuntu iso! Another iso is ok though.

I don't know if bluetooth is supported under linux yet either.

My recommendation is to get your data onto a cd, hold 'c' and boot the osx cd, before you start installing, run the disk utility and create your osx and ubuntu partitions. finish the osx install and then boot abain with the ubuntu install cd and follow their instructions.

i'd practice at least one more time before you put your data back. I think i'll be doing it a few times before i'm happy. also, when you install osx, do a custom install and deselect the extra gig's of languages and themes if you don't want them. also, you don't need IE and MS-Office demo's.

I later installed another osx on a partition and now yaboot is bypassed, so i'll have to fix that.

### Post by Len on 2005-04-11
I'd definetly stick with Mac OS X, preferably 10.2 or 10.3. All versions before were just previews. Although PPC linux works much faster on Mac as on Intel hardware, it still works slower as Mac OS X and Ubuntu with Gnome will eat 200 MB of your RAM, that's even more as OS X does. If you can get an old machine (G3) with 192 MB RAM or more OS X will run fine on that. Faster as Gnome, and much more user friendly.

OS X isn't like windows; all applications that come with it can be trashed. A base install is about 900 MB, even less without the BSD subsystem. You can 'install' applications just by dragging them to the place you like on the harddisk. The Mac OS doesn't have a "register" or "paths" or "config files" it relies on which makes applications stand alone. In other words, if your mother in law decides to put iTunes onto the Desktop, and drags the application to there instead of making a link, it just works. This isn't for applications that have data files of course, then you'll have to move the whole folder :).

As for OS 9, it is still my favorite over Mac OS X and Linux. It just lacks a decent webbrowser and some other stuff like Java 2 because all support has been dropped for quite a while now.
It is the most easy system that ever existed though. Want to make another drive bootable? Just drag your system folder to it and name it as you like. As long as the System and Finder files are in it it will boot. Want to customize the OS? Just enable or disable extensions. Those were the times... things got only more complicated (but more stable) with that OS X that they had to base on BSD. The advantage is that nearly every interesting linux app has been ported to the MacOS though.

And for you to think mac was crap, how could you reason like that without working on it? I think Windows is crap, but that's because it is my job to repair computers and what I do is repairing Windows installations. I also think Linux isn't something for the end user yet (although Ubuntu comes close!!), but I tried over 40 linux distros on those x86 PCs. As a linux newbie it was very irritating to install applications that aren't in Synaptic, or to customize some things like adding screen resolutions or installing an ATI driver and then getting that to work.
On the other hand, if it is setup to your needs and is locked only a hardware failiure is likely to get it down, so it is perfect for companies (they often complain because it is "free" and so it can't be good, but the argument that it is virus, crash and spyware free convinces the most of them :P ) Same goes for the BSD based OS X except that it is way more friendly and has much more applications available for it, not to mention perfect support for the Mac, since Apple creates the hardware and the software, there is nothing that does unexpected things. I'd just stick with that.

EDIT: Here is a screenshot of my memory usage with just top and firefox running with GNOME (click to enlarge):
[[IMG]http://img215.echo.cx/img215/7702/memusage1rw.th.png[/IMG]](http://img215.echo.cx/my.php?image=memusage1rw.png)

EDIT 2: And this is OS X with Activity Monitor (sort of top but then with a nifty interface) and FireFox open.
[[IMG]http://img225.echo.cx/img225/2893/picture21bb.th.png[/IMG]](http://img225.echo.cx/my.php?image=picture21bb.png)
Note that Ubuntu eats over 320 MB RAM while OS X is happy with about 270 MB.
Sure, if you don't run Gnome, but go for IceWM Ubuntu will use much less, but then you won't have a nice interface ;-).
It makes me wonder why OSX and Gnome use up so much memory though. Mac OS 7.5 had the same functionality but used only 8 MB of RAM. Wintel 95 had less functionality, but it also had an interface like Gnome, and it used not much as well. Time for a 'Gnome lite' or 'Xorg lite' perhaps?

EDIT 3: Can someone please tell me why some processes are listed multiple times in Top? I really just had ONE firefox window open, and only one 'nautilus'...

### Post by chascon on 2005-04-11

The problem was posted previously on this forum under the warty related forum postings.  How do i know, I personally took part in that string.

If the problem is no longer a Panther issue, then its been fixed in an update.

As for ubuntu not being for the end user ... wow
Do you prefer to go to 30 different wesites to download 30 different apps?  Or does apt-get installl APP with the 30 apps listed once sound easier?
Does driving to the store, purchasing the apple upgrade and then doing an upgrade in the hopes that the end user chooses the correct upgrade so as to not erase their previous material sound user friendly? Or in the case of wanting to do a clean install because there are ussually issues (I recall palm desktop not syncing after the Panther upgrade, this had media coverage) that arise from --I believe it is-- "archive and install" sound friendly? I don't know about you but backing everything up in order to perform a upgrade is not user friendly to me (remember we're talking about end user here who might as well delete everything previous while they upgrade to the next apple os).  I could spend my time doing something else.  Consider the the debian way ... does performing an apt-get update, upgrade then a dist-upgrade sound friendlier than the above OS X format?  It does to me.

Also consider professional quality photo editors such as gimp, and other software such as xine, which i think render better than OS X's DVD player, support for palms which I find more reliable that palm's own stuff, support for a "made for windows only scanner" (ScanJet 2100c) hand me down that I got working thanks to sane/xsane on ubuntu-ppc.  Shall I go on?

Why someone might defend OS X in the face of facts to the contrary is called "cognitive dissonanse".  Like members of cults that are shown that their sect is corrupt or false, they choose to continue to participate and give their money away.  This is what apple based its income from in the the 80s.  It created an unquestionable loyalty which is reflective by apple commercials based on Geoarge Orwell's 1984 story.

Clearly OS X is not as friendly as you apple faithfull think it is,
Think about it.

Do away with the cognitive dissonance.

If you're wondering, yes I am a apple hardware user, I just see it a little more objectively now as I've stood away from "apple faithfull" crowd for some time now.

PS-as for OS X being optimized for apple ppc hardware over and above linux-ppc.  Give me a break.  Linux-ppc now can make use of motorola technology (AltiVec) which apple calls 'velocity engine'  as OS X on G4s do.  [http://developer.apple.com/hardware/ve/](http://developer.apple.com/hardware/ve/)
Apple has also contributed optimizations to gcc which help linux-ppc code dev.  If you have ussues with gnome using too much memory use xfce4.  I do without gnome installed.  Or if you're an end user, just install xfce4 and select it as default from gdm log on screen.  Simple.

On the other side, OS X runs slow unless you use a G5.

I also use Linux for it's outstanding filesystem support. It supports nearly every filesystem which is handy if you often need to recover files from disks out of any system. I just installed Ubuntu on my 5500, and there I use it to run FireFox and some other apps because they are not available for Mac OS 9.

### Post by Polymira on 2005-04-12
On a newer mac, installing Ubuntu on it makes no sense....

 Alright, with OS X you get a rock solid OS based on BSD. You can install X11 (off the OS X cd's) and the developer tools (gcc, etc)... you can run many linux apps on it. You get the absolute beauty of the mac ... there is no single reason to switch other then "you can"

---

### Post by DJ_Max on 2005-04-12

 It makes sense when you wanna use something different and don't have to pay for major updates. OSX Is Unix, and Ubuntu is Linux. Best of both worlds.

### Post by philcolbourn on 2005-04-13
[QUOTE=bromo33333]Really?  I downloaded the latest Ubuntu iso images to my Mac and burn them to CD with no problems - for installation into my old G3 Pismo.  Sounds like their might be some issues.  

Just a thought:  On OSX have you repaired permissions recently?[/QUOTE]
 I need to add that it is a 2005 powerbook g4 17". I have no idea what hardware differences there are between the new powerbook, the old ones and any other mac - this is my first mac!

Coming from linux and before that windows I found the mac a little different. I was a little dissapointed initially as I had very high expectations from both the hardware and the software. But, now I am much more familiar with the mac and osx and I am getting used to it.

In these areas, I think ubuntu (in particular) is ahead of all the other OSs:

1. partitioning and installation (but then I have had lots of practice)

2. networking (I like the simplicity of a text file config)

3. network printing to a wireless HP 2710 all-in-one! linux (less than 5 mins, OS-X - not sure, but the HP s/w is very buggy. XP - 800MB!!!!! what is that? To be fair, the minimum is just 300MB so it ain't that bad.

Now the big question: why can't i make an image from an audio CD? I can do a DVD, but not a CD? (using disk utility of course).

I bought the mac for the screen, video editing, and to break it. I like it, but I use my 300MhZ celeron hoary box more because it has a two button mouse (i'll have to get a fancy mouse for the mac to fix this).

### Post by ssam on 2005-04-13
mac os x supports fairly old mac quite well.
mac os x 10.3 run well on a iMac G3 400mhz. (although you should make sure it has 256 or 512 of ram, and maybe a 20gb + hard drive (the way it uses swap space means you need at least a gb or 2 free on the system partition))

i run linux on my powerbook, but only because i have a linux addiction. for most people mac os x is better than linux.

---

### Post by erkki70 on 2005-04-14
[QUOTE=ssam]mac os x supports fairly old mac quite well.
mac os x 10.3 run well on a iMac G3 400mhz. (although you should make sure it has 256 or 512 of ram, and maybe a 20gb + hard drive (the way it uses swap space means you need at least a gb or 2 free on the system partition))

i run linux on my powerbook, but only because i have a linux addiction. for most people mac os x is better than linux.[/QUOTE]

I do agree with that without a shadow of doubt! ;-) 
Running OSX 10.3 and Linux on an Imac G3 266mhz 384RAM 20Gb Hard Drive very smoothly, though it took time to get there mainly because hardware restrictions  :-) No way to compare with newest G4 or G5 but system is usable and performs as expected for most of the tasks. Also feels like OSX 10.3 improvements are also an asset for older hardware as it "feels" the OS is more responsive than 10.2 was.

BTW, I made the jump to Linux through OSX, first discovering the power of terminal, then installing Fink to get Unix/Linux ports running in X11, then once comfortable enough, switched to Debian (because at the time, no other distro would really work fine, (read, whatever you try to install which for some reason get stuck to console without launching X properly, is very hard to solve when you're a newbie without experience).

Dual boot is a great experience! IMHO, the best way to get to know better how things work, hardware, software, in brief gaining control over machine and acquire enough confidence and knowledge to make it work  _your way_ without being necessarily a geek, guru or master programmer, you name it...

And yes, gaining control over an OS is definitely addictive! (or even the feeling of it is) Now, I'm far away being a "power user", but the satisfaction I have diving deeper and deeper into versatile and customizable Linux and *Nix systems, is my precious. Learning, trial and error, discovery...

Go dual boot and free your mind! (even though it needs a little effort to achieve that, but I think it's worth it...)

Cheers,
Erkki

---

### Post by chascon on 2005-04-15
Len:
First:
About OS X being friendlier because you can simply drag and drop apps to the applications dir and avoid compiling, you're obfiscating because you might also have to build from source if there is no OS X port, as you yourself have alluded to by mentioning that there is a gcc for OS X.  There is an OS X gcc for a reason you know.  I myself have complied apps for OS X because there were no ports to OS X.

Second, not all apps are installable using the above drag and drop method.  Have you tried installing OO?  There is a installer involved and no ... its files are not all self-contained below the app icon dir.  The same goes for many other apps that use installers on OS X.  So this you defense has a hole in it.  I agree that self-contained apps are desirable but they are not unifom standard.  I've even complained about it to palm concerning their OS X desktop.  Another problem with these installers is that there is a question of the dialog window's authenticity.  You could be giving away your administrative password.  I've found myself asking why does such and such file installer wants my admin password if I know the program will work without.  I've even resorted to grabbing the file from within the package and placed it myself into the appropiate dir.  Linux doesn't have this problem, or at least I don't see how they could steal your administrative password because your typing it in via term when building something, although something could be hacked but it still would not be so seamless as it may be in OS X with a fancy dialog window giving the impression that it is kosher.

You also mention that there are a lot of linux ports to OS X.  All the OSX ported apps are avaiable from different sites.  I've known about all of these except the xine one.  The fact that these ports are increasing is great but it only confirms my point that you still have to go to 30 different sites to download 30 different apps.  In linux if the app you want is not in the repository well this is one time you have to build it by hand not 30, as what might happen in OS X anyway (unless you mean to propose that absolutly every opensource app is ported to OS X, not true).  In fact, you might not even have to do everything from scratch as there are often binaries made for the most popular distros.  As for the OS X ports, you mention them as if they work flawlessly or are usable.  Although this is changing, this is not so.  Gimp has had problems moving images thru copy and paste to and from X11 and aqua for what seems to be years, yes they say it is fixed but last I checked this was still flaky.  Not to mention X11 on OS X is not accelerated, fink does not have audio working, you yourself say that you have what sounds like accel problems playing DVDS with opensource players, etc, etc, and etc.  Besides, when I say I have been able to do a lot of things on Linux that OS X has not allowed me be do, this is true as when I did them OS X did not support these actions.  This is my point, OS X trails in usability behind gnu-linux.  To add more examples, amsn is actively working to add video to their messenger.  Do you know what?  Because OS X lacks certain libraries there will be no video support for the OS X port of amsn!  There again stands my point, OS X trails behind gnu-linux.

As for debian packaging tools being available for OS X, despite their allowing access to large reposities which is convinient ... again these are not so, at least up untill you install them, because they are not part of the default install.  For instance, you have to go out of the way to install gcc to build with fink or darwinports (not that you necessarily have to build with fink).  If you want an updated
version you have to go to the apple dev site to download, and you have to constantly do this everytime a new version comes out.  Ok easy enough, but this demonstrates that OS X is not made up to be as user friendly as linux is these days.  Considering that fink has broken on me numerous times (and yes I know how to use apt-get) and that darwinports (in my past experience with them) leaves a lot to be desired in terms of documentation (gee I seem to recall them taking a month or so to get install instructions for panther which were different than for Jaguar due to the change in shells), well no the implementation of these on OS X is not friendly.

I also ask you to look at what you just did here by suggesting that OS X is friendly because it now has linux (apt based [ie., fink]) and bsd (darwin ports) packaging tools.  Doesn't this legitimate BSD and Gnu-linux as being user friendly sinse that is where they originate  from, despite their bad implementation?

I wasn't pissed off at your 'praising' OS X, if I can put it this way, but more indignified and shocked.

As for cognitive dissonance, don't moralize my comment as its just my academic training comming out.  And it applies perfectly; I'm saying that the facts suggest otherwise despite your believing it Mac OS X being more friendlier than linux.

I will grant you some credence as far as DRI which used to freeze my old iMac and sleep on my 2004 ibook doesn't work as supposed to.  But even these are supported by fedora-ppc (supports sleep with the addition of an external repository and kernels available from there), ydl (supports sleep), mandrake (never had DRI issues with them). 

Before I forget, if a software is no available from the official repository there are often rogue repositiories, thus freeing you from having to compile.  So there, everything is not so dire when official support lags.

As for adjusting gnome menu, I don't use gnome that much but there is a utility for it.  I know I used to just cntrl-click the menu, choose add launcher, and then select the app.  Easy as pie.  Now if you want to argue that dragging and dropping the app alias to the dock is easier, well you're probably right.  But I just think this type of friendlieness adds to bloat, is excessive and unnessesary.

FYI, I've used macs for years, sinse the classic days.  Thus my conclusions are based on years of experience on both classic and OS X.

Think very different, think gnu-linux

---

### Post by Len on 2005-04-17
First of all, thanks for your time to answer :). 

> **chascon]Len:
First:
About OS X being friendlier because you can simply drag and drop apps to the applications dir and avoid compiling, you're obfiscating because you might also have to build from source if there is no OS X port, as you yourself have alluded to by mentioning that there is a gcc for OS X.  There is an OS X gcc for a reason you know.  I myself have complied apps for OS X because there were no ports to OS X.[/quote]
Indeed. But I see this more as an extra feature as a requirement concerning the huge amount of applications available for Mac OS X. 
Since Apple has chosen unix as its foundation for the OS, gcc is also the compiler used by the Apple Developer tools.

[QUOTE=chascon]Second, not all apps are installable using the above drag and drop method.  Have you tried installing OO?  There is a installer involved and no ... its files are not all self-contained below the app icon dir.[/quote]
OpenOffice is an X11 application. Actually it is like running a 'Linux' app on top of Mac OS X. You could however also use [NeoOffice](http://www.macupdate.com/info.php/id/15797), a self containable app that you can drag to your harddisk. I am still waiting for a Quartz port though...

The point is, Apple has provided a way for developers to pack their applications in an executable package that is location independant. Every Mac OS X app is built this way. Sure, if you run an X11 application you have to launch it the Unix way, via a terminal or a menu. You also have to compile it yourself, or use an "installer" if the developers had the time to include one.
I don't really see a difference between using Linux to launch OpenOffice X11 or Mac OS X.
But then again, Unix/X11 compatibility is a feature of Mac OS X, not vica versa.

[QUOTE=chascon]The same goes for many other apps that use installers on OS X.  So this you defense has a hole in it.[/quote]
There are not that many. Applications that need files to be placed outside the app package (libraries, etc) come with an installer yes, but after that the installed applications are freely movable and executable by a double-click or menu choice. I don't know why this should put a hole in my defence.

[QUOTE=chascon]I agree that self-contained apps are desirable but they are not unifom standard.[/quote]
Unfortunately not, but I don't care if it is an ACCEPTED standard or not, coming from Mac OS 9 I demand the freedom to put my things wherever I want. I have four harddisks in my computer, totalling 400 GB. I do place my applications wherever I please. Short on space? I just a few large applications to my other disk and use them from there. No need to reinstall, no need to reorganise my whole system. Just drag and drop.

[QUOTE=chascon]I've even complained about it to palm concerning their OS X desktop.[/quote]
I don't have a Palm, so I don't know what problems are with it...

[QUOTE=chascon]Another problem with these installers is that there is a question of the dialog window's authenticity.[/quote]
Should it ask "Mac OS X asks for your password?" like Gnome does? Easy to spoof I guess. I don't see the difference between Gnome asking for your password when you launch something and Mac OS X.

[QUOTE=chascon]You could be giving away your administrative password. I've found myself asking why does such and such file installer wants my admin password if I know the program will work without.[/quote]
I have noticed that some developers indeed make their installers ask for a password while appearantly not needed. I don't see a problem with that other as it is quite irritating. Synaptic asks for your password everytime, even though you do not install anything. Probably the app itself needs permissions to just launch.

[QUOTE=chascon]I've even resorted to grabbing the file from within the package and placed it myself into the appropiate dir.  Linux doesn't have this problem, or at least I don't see how they could steal your administrative password because your typing it in via term when building something, although something could be hacked but it still would not be so seamless as it may be in OS X with a fancy dialog window giving the impression that it is kosher.[/quote]
Mac OS X wouldn't be Mac OS X if you had to use the terminal. As there is no virus or spyware for OS X yet I guess the system is safe, even though it asks for your password when doing something instead of stating "Permission Denied" or "mv: Error when moving to destination". For someone to steal your password, he/she has to break in. Probably the only way is via a "trojan" that the user has to excecute themselves. Not something I would consider a possibility someone has to fear when installing an Apple update or moving something from a root owned folder.
If you are paranoid though, you could set up an application like "Little Snitch" to warn you of every outgoing connection. If you want to use the terminal you could also use "ifwp" (or whatever that firewall is called) to do the job.

[QUOTE=chascon]You also mention that there are a lot of linux ports to OS X.  All the OSX ported apps are avaiable from different sites.  I've known about all of these except the xine one.  The fact that these ports are increasing is great but it only confirms my point that you still have to go to 30 different sites to download 30 different apps.[/quote]
[http://www.macupdate.com](http://www.macupdate.com) ?
Serously, I do not see the use at all of a "Synaptic" on OS X. It restricst you to a relatively small list of applications, and you do not have anything to choose where you put the applications.
It is an approach that is only neccesary if you do not use application packages like Mac OS X does.


[QUOTE=chascon]In linux if the app you want is not in the repository well this is one time you have to build it by hand not 30, as what might happen in OS X anyway (unless you mean to propose that absolutly every opensource app is ported to OS X, not true).[/quote]
There fink comes to the rescue. And I think there are as much Mac OS X opensource apps that are not in the Ubuntu software list as there are opensource unix applications not built for Mac OS X. Even the PPC version of Ubuntu lacks a lot of packages that the i386 release has. Want to use ICEWm? No choice to build it by hand and seek out for yourself what "imlib.h" or even worse, "INSERT_FUNC_NAME_HERE" is or wait for people to add it to the Synaptic list.

[QUOTE=chascon]In fact, you might not even have to do everything from scratch as there are often binaries made for the most popular distros.  As for the OS X ports, you mention them as if they work flawlessly or are usable.  Although this is changing, this is not so.  Gimp has had problems moving images thru copy and paste to and from X11 and aqua for what seems to be years, yes they say it is fixed but last I checked this was still flaky.  Not to mention X11 on OS X is not accelerated, fink does not have audio working, you yourself say that you have what sounds like accel problems playing DVDS with opensource players, etc, etc, and etc.[/quote]
Those opensource players like VLC, etc, work nice under OS X but terrible under Ubuntu. Sorry if that wasn't clear, but I have an ATI card which is a major problem under Linux due to those terrible ATI drivers. Linux appearantly doesn't have standards at all, or ATI is just plain ignoring them to make such a crappy driver.
Alas, about copying and pasting, dragging and dropping, it works. But, sometimes flaky indeed. However, try to drag and drop something between a Gnome and KDE app. Or between a Gnome and X11 app. Mostly drag and drop in linux doesn work at all. Or take File Roller for example. Instead of expanding the archive when I FINISHED dropping it onto a folder it already starts expanding when I hit the window border, forcing me to hold my mouse for minutes.

[QUOTE=chascon]Besides, when I say I have been able to do a lot of things on Linux that OS X has not allowed me be do, this is true as when I did them OS X did not support these actions.  This is my point, OS X trails in usability behind gnu-linux.  To add more examples, amsn is actively working to add video to their messenger.  Do you know what?  Because OS X lacks certain libraries there will be no video support for the OS X port of amsn!  There again stands my point, OS X trails behind gnu-linux.[/quote]
"Well, I can't run Windows apps full featured on Linux either, because Linux doesn't have all the libraries Windows has. Therefore Linux trails behind M$ Windows."
You are serously meaning that OS X trails behind Linux because it doesn't have 100% linux compatibility? Give me a break... or explain me if I misunderstood you.

[QUOTE=chascon]As for debian packaging tools being available for OS X, despite their allowing access to large reposities which is convinient ... again these are not so, at least up untill you install them, because they are not part of the default install.  For instance, you have to go out of the way to install gcc to build with fink or darwinports (not that you necessarily have to build with fink).  If you want an updated
version you have to go to the apple dev site to download, and you have to constantly do this everytime a new version comes out.  Ok easy enough, but this demonstrates that OS X is not made up to be as user friendly as linux is these days.[/quote]
Apple Software Update automatically takes care of that updating. Fink indeed doesn't use the packages Apple installed but rather reinstall them into one folder called "sw", so if you want to trash Fink and everything you installed, you just trash the "sw" folder with it. I think that is the purpose of it, after all, you are using something you'd consider as a feature next to your OS, like Wine on Linux.



[QUOTE=chascon]Considering that fink has broken on me numerous times (and yes I know how to use apt-get) and that darwinports (in my past experience with them) leaves a lot to be desired in terms of documentation (gee I seem to recall them taking a month or so to get install instructions for panther which were different than for Jaguar due to the change in shells), well no the implementation of these on OS X is not friendly.[/quote]
No, but I haven't met a user, EVER, that desperately wanted to compile gcc4 and some other libraries using fink to build the latest source of some shady terminal application.
A user rather downloads an application and drags it to where he/she likes it to be. And since there is a shitload of Mac OS X applications available -- many more then linux has, even if you look at freeware -- I don't think a user would ever be tempted to install Fink or compile something from source.

[QUOTE=chascon]I also ask you to look at what you just did here by suggesting that OS X is friendly because it now has linux (apt based [ie., fink]) and bsd (darwin ports) packaging tools. >

### Post by slux on 2005-04-18
[QUOTE=Len]I know that. OS X is unix based and so handles RAM the same as Linux does. How could I have a 'windows' view of RAM(mis)usage if I compare OS X to Linux ;).[/QUOTE]

Bollocks. Not all Unix-like operating systems handle RAM in the same way. I am not sure about OS X, but for example FreeBSD proactively throws apps out of the system RAM.

And even in the case that they did handle it in the exact same way, your comparison is absolutely meaningless because the method always tries to use as much memory as possible. You can easily fill the whole memory with cached data in Linux even if you had 4GB of memory, given enough time. If you can see a difference here it's probably because of less activity on the system in the past.

If you want to compare memory usage, you'll have to look at different numbers than the overall usage reported by top. It's a bit tricky to get accurate numbers because most applications use shared libraries so for example the RSS column in ps will report larger numbers than actually used by the application.

---

### Post by chascon on 2005-04-19
You have some good points, and for once I agree with you on ever point.  But I think where we differ is on what we trust and emphasize because of our worldview and perhaps our differing ideologies.

But let meddress one important difference:
"So what's the difference for installing an app on Linux with root permissions, or doing that on OS X? In both cases you could be tricked. For me it is the same. Fully."

There is a fundamental difference between the two if we draw between, at least open source and proprietary: 
The difference is the differece between benefitting from peer reviewed and improved including concerning security holes (ie., trojans) and the lack of thereof in the propietary world.  Thus the software you install using passwords in apt-get have a lower risk of being stolen because the software has been peer reviewed, not only for quality but, for malicious code.

Now if you want to argue that fink or opendarwin also benefit from this, fine but this is not optimized for OS X.  ie., No accel. And this probably does not make up the majority of OS X software.  Thus we have a bluring of the two opposite models.  And OS X benefits from this bluring as it incorporates/adapts to its own agenda the opensource movement.  So now we have darwin which has been released publically.  Still, it still incorporates the weaker propietary model.  In the end when you install OS X software, regardless of password or not although worse if you use admin passwords, you do not benefit from peers reviewing source code to see if it is malicious.

In the end, with OS X propietary software, dragable or installer driven, you start from a position of disadvantage.  You can't get away from this.

---

### Post by slux on 2005-04-22
> 
Well, Apple has opensourced the base system, Darwin, so yes, everyone can benefit from that. They won't *ever* opensource their system though for several reasons, most important two:
1. Apple wants to be known to provide superior quality, everything 'just works'. If they opensource their system they'll lose quality control; it will probably be ported to other architectures immediately with all concequences as half functioning drivers and bugs following.
Since Apple doesn't do much marketing on their OS it is clear that they rely on the user base to spread the Macintosh. They do not want people to get a bad impression by seeing Mac OS X crawl forward on an X86 box.
2. The main reason to buy a Macintosh is the Mac OS. The fast hardware is really not that important. If it is ported to other platforms they'll lose their main income.

Unfortunately a base UNIX system is nothing short of a completely useless contribution. We have GNU/Linux, we have the BSDs. They're a commodity and Apple can only gain themselves from it. As for the basis you're trying to give for their actions, 

1. I don't see happening with other free software so I don't buy it. It's the same that Sun-fans say is the reason they can't make the JRE free software. There are ways. Most projects seem to get away just fine without any sort of precautions but Mozilla Firefox and Red Hat easily protect themselves from this by disallowing the use of their trademarks in unofficial builds that do not meet certain criteria.  Only versions that have quality control carry the name.

2.  The reason I've bought macs is certainly not the software. For many people the software probably does play a role but I'd argue that it's the whole package, quality hardware with beautiful design combined with a reliable operating system and application suite that empowers creativity.  And just like you said, OS X wouldn't work well on non-apple hardware anyway so the whole point is moot by your first argument.

I just replace the OS with another one that has pros and cons compared to OS X, some similar qualities  and works better for me.

If Macs had continued to look the way they looked thru the 80's and first half of the 90's but still had incorporated OS X the way it is today I doubt we'd see the surge of interest in Apple that it has enjoyed since Jobs returned. It's very much about the hardware while all the pre-iMac features and OS X did help.

> 
Yes, but it is not nearly the amount of stuff available for the Macintosh, and also only a part of what is available for Linux. Actually Linux should standardize itself. Now you have hundreds of distros, incompatible with each other, requiring each distro maintainer to provide their own list. Ubuntu tries to ease the maintaining by being Debian based.
But you can't tell me that there is no universal system possible to address this problem.
What I desperately would see in Desktop Linux is the following:

I doubt that a little and even if true, most of the software available for the mac you'll have to pay for so you're never going to get it all unless you're rich.

I'll tell you the reason why there's no possibility of a universal system:
Different people like different things. Personally I like Ubuntu a lot but I also like Arch, which is a completely different system. There are several other distributions that I see some redeeming qualities in. There is no universal solution and there won't be. What CAN be done is try to set up some common standards to do certain things so interoperability is better.

> An alternative to X11. I always have problems with it and it is bloated, really bloated. Xorg is a nice try, but why not an Xorg Lite or so? FireFox is also based off Mozilla. 

In what way is it bloated? People who say this have usually come to this opinion by wrongly interpreting the ps output of it's memory usage. The number you see includes at least all the pixmaps of all the running programs. X is fine, back when it was invented it couldn't afford to be that bloated and it's still used on some embedded devices, I believe some kind of a "light" version does even exist. Network transparency is a unique feature.

> 
A light version of Gnome. Mac OS 7 provides more functionality as Gnome and the whole system only uses 5 MB of RAM. Gnome could aim for the 30 MB, but 90+ for just drawing a few windows...

I wouldn't say it provides more functionality. I do accept that most of the basic functionality is there and it still seems like a huge difference considering that. The improved graphics is one one suspect, they tend to consume space uncompressed. Classic MacOS memory management is also extremely primitive and that lets it get away with less while not being all that stable or flexible. OS7 didn't have true multitasking either. Not to say X or GNOME doesn't have some overhead too, but there are many factors to consider and I don't see OS X or XP faring much better than GNOME, in fact XP is significantly worse and OS X about the same.

> 
Also, if I work in Gnome or KDE I feel tied. They should add way more graphical configuration apps. Something to configure X, something to configure the bootloader, something to finally quickly switch monitor bit depth, etc. Doing all this in the terminal is not only very unfriendly, but it is also a helluva lot more work.
Of course there are numerous other things to think off, but these are the most irritating for me.

It's happening as we speak. The configuration tools first appeared in GNOME 2.8 (or I first noticed them then) and they've been improved upon in 2.10. For now, you can just use a distribution that provides their own control center, independent of the desktop environment you're using. Such distributions are for example Mandriva (ex-Mandrake) and SUSE. Ubuntu has started making some distro specific tools as well, but I hope they're not getting into the control center game. The distributions should've originally started collaborating on the kind of solution GNOME is coming up with now but they often tend to resort to "quick hacks".

Now you have to realize that making such a system isn't as easy as it is for Apple to ship some tools in OS X. GNOME has to take into account not only all the differences between various Linux distributions, but also other free and proprietary UNIX-like systems such as *BSD and Solaris. There are multiple X servers available too (Xorg, XFree86, Solaris). It's nice to be cross-platform but it makes things often complicated.

There is just, in my eyes, so much work being done for about 100 times instead 1. That manpower (and that's about 90% I guess) could be used way better.

[QUOTE=slux]In what way is it bloated? People who say this have usually come to this opinion by wrongly interpreting the ps output of it's memory usage. So Gnome not only uses memory itself, but also loads all those 32 bit PNGs with mask into the memory of X... I should adapt my 90 MB then. X11 with IceWM also still uses around the 20 MB though, and the harddisk footprint is rather big for something that supposedly just has to draw windows. I believe drag and drop is managed by the windowmanager.

[QUOTE=slux]I wouldn't say it provides more functionality. I do accept that most of the basic functionality is there and it still seems like a huge difference considering that. The improved graphics is one one suspect, they tend to consume space uncompressed. Classic MacOS memory management is also extremely primitive and that lets it get away with less while not being all that stable or flexible. OS7 didn't have true multitasking either.[/quote]
No, but should multitasking and protected memory increase the requirements by 2000%? I certainly hope not. Mac OS 7 didn't have a 32 bit image interface, but that's actually the least I care for.  With 8 bits and 1 bit masks you can do amazing things as well. And the functionality it had is awesome. Just everything you insert is mounted on the desktop, every application interacts with each other, so dragging and dropping sound files to SimpleText was possible because it used the Quicktime plugin, every application uses the top screen menu instead of their own, you have access to your full harddrive, you can drop extensions into the "Extensions" folder in your system and they just work, you have a nice application switcher instead of a "taskbar", etc, etc ,etc. 
Gnome, which provides a taskbar with some Apple menu parody, a desktop with a trash and tries to automount volumes looks very bleak to that, yet it has about 100 times the memory requirement. Mac OS 7 without extensions loaded comes under the 2 MB in memory usage...

[QUOTE=slux]Not to say X or GNOME doesn't have some overhead too, but there are many factors to consider and I don't see OS X or XP faring much better than GNOME, in fact XP is significantly worse and OS X about the same.[/quote]
Although Mac OS X has a slightly less feature rich 'window manager' as the old Mac OS I think it is still miles ahead of Gnome. Gnome evolves quickly though...

[QUOTE=slux]It's happening as we speak. The configuration tools first appeared in GNOME 2.8 (or I first noticed them then) and they've been improved upon in 2.10. For now, you can just use a distribution that provides their own control center, independent of the desktop environment you're using. Such distributions are for example Mandriva (ex-Mandrake) and SUSE. Ubuntu has started making some distro specific tools as well, but I hope they're not getting into the control center game. The distributions should've originally started collaborating on the kind of solution GNOME is coming up with now but they often tend to resort to "quick hacks".[/quote]
Well, they could start contributing to Gnome instead of producing their own control center? I'm well aware of the development. I check back every now and then without any prejudice or memories about my previous experiences with the different distros.
I wonder why those tools are not included from the 1.0 release, I mean, a windowmanager is meant to attract desktop users that do not like the terminal right?

[QUOTE=slux]Now you have to realize that making such a system isn't as easy as it is for Apple to ship some tools in OS X. GNOME has to take into account not only all the differences between various Linux distributions, but also other free and proprietary UNIX-like systems such as *BSD and Solaris. There are multiple X servers available too (Xorg, XFree86, Solaris). It's nice to be cross-platform but it makes things often complicated.[/QUOTE]
Yes, it is time to organise some stuff, where possible. There must be at least some 'standard' for things that are needlessly complicated. And although the developers try to be compatible with everything, the user often has the trouble. Why isn't a list of required libraries included in most readme files for example?

>

> 

The point wasn't that Red Hat is particularly good. It was that releasing your source code under a free license does not prevent you from exercising quality control so it is a poor excuse for staying proprietary. Besides, Fedora isn't Red Hat. RHEL is.

 
[QUOTE]
If you use it as a desktop system, I'm interested in the pros it has over OS X. I can imagine the pros for a server, or utility machine, but desktop?


We've talked about such benefits in this thread previously. Finding an application for a specific purpose and installing it is easier than it is in any other OS. Keeping those up to date is equally painless. 

After installing a distribution you already have a clean selection of applications for most tasks you want to perform while on OS X you'll have to go hunting for some (and possibly even buy a few). 

The quality of the desktop apps is, in my personal opinion, excellent. I find K3B, AmaroK, OpenOffice.org, Mozilla Firefox, Evolution, Mplayer and many others to be better than the alternatives offered on other platforms and they are all installed out of the box.

The OS tends to stay in a consistent state when all components are centrally managed, upgrading to the very latest versions is free (as in beer). The desktop works well (a matter of theand gives me (fast) virtual desktops. There's more but I think I've covered the major ones. These are just the instantly visible practical benefits.

**<edit>**Actually, I also listed some reasons in [This post](http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=137510&postcount=24) . The openness is a big one I missed on this one.**</edit>**

[QUOTE]
Half of the Mac software is free. Others are mostly shareware; e.g. you have to pay for it when you want all features. Also, most Linux apps are ported over to OS X.

Shareware usually means you can try it for a limited period... Can you name a few major free (as in beer) software packages for the mac? I'm not completely familiar with the application library of the platform.
As for the "ported Linux apps", Apple X11 leaves a lot to be desired and the programs aren't well integrated to the desktop. They are a lot better on a true X11 platform. And I seriously doubt it's really the case that "most Linux apps" are. Fink or Darwinports don't seem to be as comprehensive as you make them out to be.

> 
Well, Arch uses the same libraries as all those other distributions. I don't see many problems there. If they optimize their stuff for the i686, nice, but that means universal apps could still run on it.
There is just, in my eyes, so much work being done for about 100 times instead 1. That manpower (and that's about 90% I guess) could be used way better.


Arch has a simple, completely different packaging format and managers. They try to keep the system simple enough and easily configurable by hand and leave what they consider useless stuff (/usr/doc mostly) out of the distribution. What libraries or binary formats they happen to use really isn't the issue here.

> 
So Gnome not only uses memory itself, but also loads all those 32 bit PNGs with mask into the memory of X... I should adapt my 90 MB then. X11 with IceWM also still uses around the 20 MB though, and the harddisk footprint is rather big for something that supposedly just has to draw windows. I believe drag and drop is managed by the windowmanager.


That is incorrect. The images are shared between X and the Apps (X has to get to them somehow after all) so the actual memory usage is much less than you think when you add together what GNOME seems to use and what X seems to use in ps or top. See this [mailing list post by Miguel de Icaza](http://mail.gnome.org/archives/gnome-list/1999-April/msg02337.html) for an explanation.

> Yes, it is time to organise some stuff, where possible. There must be at least some 'standard' for things that are needlessly complicated. And although the developers try to be compatible with everything, the user often has the trouble. Why isn't a list of required libraries included in most readme files for example?

List of required libraries isn't much of a desktop user issue, but I haven't really run into many apps that don't have proper READMEs and INSTALLs documenting what is needed and ./configure complains if something's missing in a very clear manner.

[QUOTE=slux]List of required libraries isn't much of a desktop user issue, but I haven't really run into many apps that don't have proper READMEs and INSTALLs documenting what is needed and ./configure complains if something's missing in a very clear manner.[/QUOTE]
It depends on how that configure script is setup. Often it doesn't check for all libs required, resulting in cryptic compile errors, it can notice you of ".... not found" without telling which package/bundle this file belongs to, and at last it does stop on the first fault. Instead it makes an error report... this way you keep installing packages without seeing the end.
The INSTALL often only describes that you have to type "./configure && make && sudo make install", nothing else, and the readme describes what the file is. No dependencies list... This could be a desktop user issue since many applications that exist are not in the list yet.

### Post by farruinn on 2005-04-27

For the record, I agree with most people in this thread, Linux is great for older Mac hardware, but if you're getting a new computer I don't see any reason to install Linux.  OS X has plenty of *nix for me.  However, I also find Mac hardware well supported simply because Apple is the only company that makes these computers so there are a limited number of models which makes supporting Macs easier.

Lets see now, Quartz extreme, accel as far as I can tell, is not supported on my iMac DV, although it runs Panther.  Yet, Ubuntu supports accel on this machine.  So who has better support?  Now also factor in that OS X gave up all support for old world machines, and that gnu-liux suports many of them, one distro or another, hmm are you sure OS X has better support for their own macines?  OF course the counter bantter would be ... but linux does not support airport extreme, or my modem.  I believe this is because apple realizes that gnu-linux supports their machines better, thus the placing of obstacles to impede gnu-linux support.  These the manufacturers of such machines were supportive, yes gnu-linux would have better support for these hardware pieces without a doubt.

 
And OS X is not based on gnu-linux, it is a unix-like OS with a lot of 'stolen' ideas from gnu and other places (such as the BSDs).

Opinion:

You have to realize, x86 Linux users have a much better time with manufacturer cooperation (granted it's not perfect).  They have graphics drivers and the official flash plugin at least.  The only reason why these things don't exist for PPC linux users is that there are *so few* people using Linux on PPC hardware.  Think about it, how likely is it that the user base of PPC linux is going to be very large if Apple has a "market share" <=10%?

### Post by slux on 2005-04-28
> 
Well, I use Linux and give nothing back. Seriously though, if Apple uses open source stuff and submit all he changes they made I think they can't give more back.


They're using free software to form a significant base of their OS. On top of that, in many cases they are legally obliged to submit the changes they make in the first place.

Most of the real work they are doing with OS X is not in the free parts but still builds on them. As far as I'm concerned, providing some minor patches to what you're using is not giving back much at all.

> 
RHEL costs more as Mac OS X, nothing free about that. The fedora source doesn't contain any source of the additions made to RHEL. Same with OS X; Darwin is free, OS X you have to pay for. And another very good reason to ask money for software is that some developers need to eat.

Not nearly the same. The only thing not free about RHEL is the Red Hat branding. There are ***zero*** software packages that you can't get the source for inside it. Look up CentOS, White Box Linux or a similar distro for a demonstration of that. And I thought we were talking about quality control, not business models.

> 
OS X has most tools aboard: Quicktime, Mail, Safari, iTunes, DVD Player, and everyone that bought a Mac the last five years has Appleworks and iLife (iPhoto, iMovie, iDVD). Is there any good video editing software for Linux by the way?


Doesn't come with an Office suite though. Or a image editing app. Or a 3D modeller.
iLife is a nice suite of apps but they're directed towards the home users and as far as iMovie and Garageband go, that makes their usefulness somewhat limited to more advanced users.

I don't use video editing apps much so I can't really comment but I think chascon was thinking of Film GIMP, now renamed [Cinepaint](http://cinepaint.sourceforge.net/) 

> 
As I mentioned before, I do prefer freedom of placement over "package management", what else should I use other hard disks for, documents? And I do not like to be restricted to a package list (well, the alternative is building from source) as well. Mac OS X updates also can update 'any part of the system'. Even Windows can do that.


Not being able to freely throw application binaries around the filesystem does not mean you can't use other hard disks for storing application binaries. The GNU/Linux filesystem can be divided on as many partitions as you desire and after you've ran out of separate directories to place on different partitions you can still use symlinks. Furthermore, using things like LVM/EVMS you can combine separate hard disks to a single virtual disk and get rid of arbitrary constraints placed by the physical setup of your hardware. The UNIX directory structure isn't based on disk drives anyway, be it OS X or GNU/Linux unlike *that third OS*. I like the idea that users only organize their data and the applications are in predefined locations, but that's just me. I guess both ways have their pros and cons.

By "any part of the system" I mean't **any and all applications installed on the computer**. Windows and MacOS won't update my 3rd party apps.

> 
As for the ideology, I do not care, and even Linus seems to not care. Otherwise he wouldn't use BitKeeper. If something that isn't free works better, I pay for it if it is affordable to what it offers.

(later post)
No, recently not anymore because he was harassed by open source zealots:
[http://kerneltrap.org/node/4968](http://kerneltrap.org/node/4968)
I can only imagine the amount of wasted time.


You're both entitled to that opinion of course. Not that agreeing with Linus gives any more weight to your opinion. I agree with Stallman but so what?

As for the Bitkeeper case, "being harrassed by open source zealots" may be McVoy's or Linus' view of what happened but I don't think that's the popular one. Tridgell was well within his rights "reverse engineering" BitKeeper, no way around it. McVoy seems to be the more likely villain here and my respect for Linus dropped a lot when he sided with him.

> 
Shareware sometimes has a time limit, but more often it has some "registered only" functions or an annoying nag window. If you use it much you often consider to pay the price. Sometimes it is just $5 anyway. If you occasionally need it, or do not need the extra stuff then why register?

I've seldom ran into shareware that didn't offer only a limited trial period. Whether the trial version keeps working after that period has passed or not is irrelevant, it is still illegal to use it.

> 
I noticed that while trying to drag text to FireFox in Linux I got source code, and dragging or copying an image is out of the question. X11 applications are not even compatible with their own kind. I had to try three times to copy my "top" list to "Gedit" from the Gnome Terminal. I told it to "Copy", Gedit to "Paste" and voila: nothing. 3rd time all seemed to work OK, and this is no mistake. It was a freshly booted system with nothing on the clipboard yet, so the "Paste" menu item would have been greyed out if I accidently didn't copy the stuff.

Yeah, the clipboard functionality in GNU/Linux is not perfect. It works for copying basic text but not much more. Luckily 90% of pasting for me is text. It's true that the remaining 10% still is an inconvenience. 

> 
As I noted before, X11 is an optional feature, almost comparable to wine on Linux. Both systems differ more as that they share, so why does everyone expects it to run Linux apps natively, and expect Quartz/Aqua to be fully compatible with them? Sure, you can install Gnome and KDE on OS X as well, but that doesn't mean that OS X = Linux. I do not even know why these are ported over... I'd rather have some nice polished Aqua builds of some opensource stuff still running with X11.

You misunderstood me. I don't expect to run them "natively". I just pointed out I can't and view that as a disadvantage. This has nothing to do with how reasonable it is to expect such functionality.

> 
That's nice, but that still doesn't explain why other binaries or packages can't be compatible. If something isn't available for Arch you wouldn't mind if it came from another system and installs some useless stuff. It could be trimmed for Arch later. You also use some KDE stuff under Gnome until it has been ported over to Gnome, you would already be happy that it works, so why not do the same with packages from different distributions? One standard package format or list could allow commercial software companies in which could finally provide some of the better stuff like pro audio, video, DTP, DVD, etc apps.


Well, I don't find myself needing such a thing very often but I guess [Autopackage](http://www.autopackage.org/) is what you want. Just installing binaries right and left across distributions is a pipe dream that isn't trivially solvable. GNU/Linux uses a lot of shared libs and that's the reason there's a need for package managers. Using shared libraries is good because it saves memory and work.

> 
Jeez, is it so hard for top to be clear? Anyway, by counting up the 'res' values I still get a huge amount. Here is my top output (I opened NO windows and just ONE terminal, although top seems to think I have 3 open or so). Also, some processes use no memory. Yeah, right.


The processes that don't use memory belong to the kernel and are not regular processes running in user space. The memory usage numbers aren't applicable to them. Your huge number apparently comes from adding together all instances of for example nautilus in the list. Notice that they all have the same amount of memory reported as being used. They are actually all using just the 11MB in total, one is the parent process of others and they share the same memory space. The usage of child processes is also the reason for you having 3 gnome-terminal processes. Now if you don't like the info GNU top gives you, maybe you should use GNOME's system monitor. It's in Applications->System->System monitor. If you go to the resources tab there you can even get a pretty nice memory usage report. It's reporting 105MB for me right now on my iBook with a gnome terminal and XChat open.

> 
This could be a desktop user issue since many applications that exist are not in the list yet.

No. It absolutely is not. If an author of an application wants it to have users, he'll make it available in a easier format than a source tarball & compilation instructions. If the system provides easy methods of installation but the author opts not to use them, it's not the system's fault. Someone may be offering OS X apps as a source tarball also. Why don't you consider it a OS X usability issue?

> Also, about stealing, didn't Apple invent the whole desktop environment users work with today?

Completely off-topic, but no they did not. Apple did (arguably) make it popular and offer it to home users a decade before Microsoft which is significant but Xerox PARC came up with the basic concepts. [Wikipedia article on graphical user interfaces](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphical_user_interface). Look, I like many things that Apple does but your attitude seems like one of a Apple zealot. GNU/Linux has it's flaws but it's a nice system and I happen to rate it over OS X overall. The same goes for you and OS X.

### Post by chascon on 2005-04-28
> I'm sure I'll regret saying this, but what "stolen ideas"? Isn't GNU itself is a bunch of "stolen ideas" given that it was designed to create an open source clone of Unix? So technically, one could argue (please note, I'm not arguing this point, just acting as devil's advocate) that Linux is stealing from OS X. 

No first of all GNU is not stolen as it is a write from scratch.  It's not even a rewrite.  Stallman never wrote GNU code based on unix, nor has he ever, AFAIK, seen it.  Certainly, he has never seen it while writing GNU.  

No BSDs are interesting because they are rewrites of unix.  Unix code was so aweful that Berkley rewrote the whole thing, or so they thought they did.  They were taken to court for distributing their rewrite by people who thought they owned Berkley's OS.  After years of legal procedings they came ot an agreement.  That the propietary code would be taken out.  This became BSD lite.  So as it stands today, nothing has been 'stolen' from unix, by either by BSD or GNU.

Now on the other hand, lets look at apple OS X:
Do you think that the open ssh or Emacs app you use on OS X has been rewriten by Apple?  (There are numerous apps this could apply to.)  Think about it.  I dont think so.

### Post by slux on 2005-04-29
Well, we have a bit extreme opinions both ways here it seems. Apple isn't all bad but they're not a saint either.

I doubt they're actively trying to cripple Linux on Mac hardware with their choices regarding the Airport extreme. Most drivers for Darwin being closed does make it even less usable than it already would be with all drivers intact though. 

I think Apple's just using free software components because they've realized that they can avoid some of the work and costs involved in creating and maintaining an operating system that way. They're not very interested in participating in the community and they're seeking advantages by keeping their self-developed technology closed. That's fine but I don't like hearing from the Apple fanatics how Apple's so pro- open source. They're not. Eventually, I think they're going to have to be, just like Microsoft but that's still years away.

It's easy to like Apple. They make sexy hardware and software, are the long-time underdog of the computer industry that was always better than the other option. Everything they do tries to convey the message that they think out of the box. They have a lot of cool stuff and have certainly done more with free software than Microsoft.

But don't get blinded by them. Most of the Mac users I see posting on message boards are fanatic enough about their platform of choice that the suggestion of installing Linux on a Mac is blasphemy to them. People tend to get passionate about these things but most Windows users aren't as zealous, GNU/Linux gets it's fair share of fundamentalists too but I don't see the kind of mass following Apple enjoys. Don't mistake this for idealism, BTW. The pragmatist elements in the open source community often like to write the free sofware movement off as a bunch of fanatics but it's not blind faith to ask what is ethical and then act accordingly. 

Nothing's perfect. Moreover, not everyone likes dark chocolate or any one operating system. It's best to acknowledge the flaws that your platform of choice has and also understand that someone may dislike a feature you happen to consider a good thing. Spreading FUD about other systems doesn't help either, it's just going to make you look less credible.

---

I even have an email from a broadcom employee stating that they are not set up or have the resources to help end users, and I take it he also meant to free source community because that was the point of my emails.  Gee how hard is it to set up a server from which the linux community could download the source themselves.  From there, the free community could provide their own support to users.   You don't have to have the manpower to assist every single user.  You just provide great documentation and the code and you never have to deal with a single user.  This way the manpower, if any is needed because you probably already have it all documented, is exerted only once.  Eventually you have to come to the acceptance that people do not want linux support to exist.  Why? This gives them a market edge on that hardware.  The fact that this gives a market edge is undeniable considering they control what OSes can run said hardware.

I'm aware of the Broadcom situation as I have been following it for a year or two now. It's unfortunate but it's not even nearly the only case. I recently bought a ADSL router with built-in 802.11g WLAN from D-link. It runs on Linux and has a whole bunch of binary-only driver modules - for the ADSL chip, the WLAN chip etc. These are made by Texas Instruments.

No-one seems to be willing to pick up the binary module mess and they're fast becoming a problem as the embedded market grows. Even Linus thinks that most of them are illegal but the kernel team isn't apparently interested in forcing source releases and few others are in the position to do so. The issue is a little fuzzy anyway as there's no court decision concerning the GPL and dynamic linking and it's hard to tell what the outcome would be.

I wish [GPL violations](http://www.gpl-violations.org/) or [Software Freedom Law Center](http://www.softwarefreedom.org/) would do something with this but it seems unlikely.

Apple still hasn't got much to do with the Linux driver that has been developed for the Airport Extreme, that's probably developed by Broadcom itself.I do consider it possible that Apple would purposefully select a piece of hardware that does not work with Linux but they haven't done this with other hardware - their current line of laptops very conveniently have Radeon video cards while Nvidia would be the less well supported option for GNU/Linux for example.

My guess is that Apple doesn't feel Linux is a threat on their platform at all (for now?). After all, most Mac users are very faithful and they sell a version of Mac OS X with every single new Macintosh anyway. The Airport extreme is an unfortunate choice by them but I am aware of only one 802.11g chipset that would be possible to support on PPC Linux currently so it isn't that difficult to choose one that isn't supported if you're just not taking the matter into consideration at all.




> 
Yes but getting passionate to the point where gnu-linux defenders are refered to as trolls?
And that on a gnu-linux forum?  Seems entirely wrong.

Trolling doesn't really have much to do with what the stated opinion is or where one's doing it. Still, I think the term isn't apt for anyone here posting on the thread (well I certainly hope so anyway) as everyone is expressing their genuine opinion instead of just trying to provoke angry responses. It's been getting fairly close to degrading into an all-out flamewar though.

Now you might say, but what interest does apple have in locking people into their OS when they are a hardware company, not a software company.  They have allways been a software company, partially.  Afterall, they have always had Oses.  I believe that there was a transition to being more a software company when apple started charging regularly for updates (which incidently are already subsidized by free public R&D Darwin).  Just to be clear, the updates I refer to are Jaguar to Panther and so on, as there are sufficient articles on the net explaining away these upgrades as updates.

Apple is certainly obligated to try to keep its hardware users on OS X (thru airport extreme), else apple looses its software developers, and attraction to their products in the first place.  Thus, apple is obligated by financial pressures to attempt to lock users into OS X, because if they lose their OS X user base, developers loose interest in apple and the apple suffers financially.  Isn't the apple developer convention in part about convincing developers to release software for OS X, not only because OS X is supposed to facilatate development but because they have a large loyal following?  I recall Jobs making a point to tell developers at one convention a few years ago about how they've succesfully migrated the majority of their users to OS X, and how no other OS company has been able to migrate their user base off of their older OSes like apple has.  Let me put it this way, why would a developer bother to write software for apple's OS if their user base is migrating to linux-ppc?  Or if the ppc user base is divided between linux and OS X users.

Now you could argue that gnu-linux is not enough of a threat for apple to notice.  If it isn't, would it not still be better to nip thing in the bud rather than later after one has lost significant market share?

Besides gnu-linux's acceptance by governments, schools, and more all over the the globe over propietary OSes demonstrates that it is a very real threat.  It's enough of a threat for Gates to have flown over to china and promised the Chinese government the source to windows ([http://news.zdnet.com/2100-3513_22-990526.html)](http://news.zdnet.com/2100-3513_22-990526.html)), for Gates to allude to GPL and its proponesnts as communists, etc.  Remember the m$ memo that was leaked calling linux a cancer ([http://www.techweb.com/wire/26800386)?](http://www.techweb.com/wire/26800386)?)  I don't see a reason why apple should be impervious and ignorant to these economical realites and potentialities, and then not do anything about.  I don't think they are stupid.

---

### Post by chascon on 2005-05-01
> 
Apple isn't in the same position as Sun. At all. Sun did the whole server/enterprise thing. Linux is a good server OS. Apple primarily does the whole desktop shebang, something which Linux lacks at the moment. If (and that's a big if) linux's desktop environment improved up to OS X standard, then there would obviously be a competition between Linux and OS X. 

Well yeah, linux is known as a desktop, as opposed to BSDs that are primarily known for servers.  It's not where OS X is quite yet, with certain bells and whistles, but its fully functional for everything I want it to do (and thus I expect meets other people's desktop needs) and I was an apple OS user for many years before using gnu-linux.  So what I'm saying is that gnu-linux already offers a competitive desktop environment.  On the other hand, I believe that gnu-linux has had features before apple has incorporated them.  The non-spatial windowing that Panther has is heavily reminicent of kde for instance.  I'd be guessing but I'd be willing to bet that gnu-linux desktop environments had the ability to save to ps before apple provided the ability to save to pdf.  
> Linux is a good server OS 
On the chance of sounding like I've just written an athema, Linux is not a good server, at least not traditionally.  BSDs, with all their unfriendly, elitist culture as I've found with Freebsd and obsd (netbsd excepted), traditionaly take this credit. Althought linux kernel 2.6 may start to change attitudes.  Still, if you look at the the major high traffic sites, they usually use BSDs.  I believe FBSD had a long standing record for the largest processing of traffic.

If I accept your stance that apple is primarily a desktop ... well I agree.  To add to this, apple is looking to get into the server side of things.  They do make a server version of their OS you know; If linux is more of a server as you say and apple is getting into servers then they are going to clash on yet another genre.

I gather what is happening is that both apple and gnu-linux are converging.  They are meeting on the same grounds but do so from different points of origin.  Gnu-linux is coming from a non-graphical environment ('server' if you want), and apple OS that is comming from a graphical environment.  Both are moving into each others traditionally held environments, and in doing so they are threatening each other.  So don't be fooled with the simpleton argument that apple is only concerned with the desktop environment and that gnu-linux with servers.  Ask yourelf, did you install ubuntu because you wanted a server?  I doubt it.  If you'd wanted a server, you wouldn't have installed debian (if you like gnu-linux for servers else BSD) because ubuntu is bleeding edge.  If you're a responsible admin you install older proven secure software not bleeding edge stuff, unless you like to take chances on security and like to have pissed off employers breathing down your neck when things go wrong.  Think about it.  Is Ubuntu-ppc a server distro? Or a desktop environment based distro?

On the note of sun being more about servers, if it was it certainly isn't limited to servers now:
[http://shots.osdir.com/slideshows/slideshow.php?release=215&slide=18](http://shots.osdir.com/slideshows/slideshow.php?release=215&slide=18)
Also, if you view [http://webcast-east.sun.com/archives/GSN-1312/GSN-1312_forjds.mov](http://webcast-east.sun.com/archives/GSN-1312/GSN-1312_forjds.mov)
you'll see looking glass (2003) was, if I have my timeline correct, years ahead of apple's desktop.  OS X just recently, with Panther, developed this 3D cube trick when setting up an account with the Panther install.  I mean we're talking turning items around in 3D, just mind boggling things for a desktop.  Evenso, [http://www.macnewsworld.com/story/34271.html](http://www.macnewsworld.com/story/34271.html) argues that linux took a bite out their (sun's) business.

That sun demo is from [http://www.sun.com/software/looking_glass/demo.xml](http://www.sun.com/software/looking_glass/demo.xml)
and screen shots
[http://www.sun.com/software/looking_glass/details.xml](http://www.sun.com/software/looking_glass/details.xml)

As already explained, the community doesn't benefit from propietary drivers especially. This is only reasonable because I doubt apple bought hardware for which they had to reverse engineer drivers.  This would be inpractical.  Apple could easily provide the source for their drivers, and they would be legally able to do it because the code is all estimation based on/or GPL code, and thus subject to GPL.  Thus, apple is not as a benign as one might think.  I propose that they're obviously holding back.[/QUOTE]

I think something like this must be behind the fact that all the different router manufacturers have refused to release the modules in unison, I'd lay the majority of the blame on Broadcom and some on the vendors using the chips. Unless they're willing to go fighting for the free software movement in this matter, which doesn't seem to be the case.
