I just feel it's much more of a threat on the x86 side. Most people don't see any reason to get a Mac as they apparently don't care about the nice hardware. I suspect the percentage of Mac owners running Linux is significantly smaller compared to the percentage of PC users.

I'm just not yet convinced that Apple has a malicious intent towards GNU/Linux. What I am convinced of is that their relation with free software is mostly parasitic. (they do make some valuable contributions, though). They've historically done worse things such as preventing BeOS from being developed for the G3s (by not releasing the specs), not that I think they could do that for Linux. It does sound frighteningly similar to what the situation with Airport Extreme is though.

[QUOTE=Chunga]

I hear this all the time. Go back something like 2 years and it was true in many places but GNOME is really getting pretty good. These days I mostly hear about installing new hardware (non-USB kind) and software installation. 

The first one doesn't really apply to most Macs, involves relatively complicated procedures (physical installation) and is solved by some distributions already anyway. That and the vast majority of users will never do it themselves.

 The second complaint is common just because they way it's done on GNU/Linux is different (and in my opinion superior).

### Post by slux on 2005-05-01
[QUOTE=chascon]
On the chance of sounding like I've just written an athema, Linux is not a good server, at least not traditionally.  BSDs, with all their unfriendly, elitist culture as I've found with Freebsd and obsd (netbsd excepted), traditionaly take this credit. Althought linux kernel 2.6 may start to change attitudes.  Still, if you look at the the major high traffic sites, they usually use BSDs.  I believe FBSD had a long standing record for the largest processing of traffic.
[/QUOTE]

Not that it relates much to the current discussion but I have to object to that. GNU/Linux is an excellent server and really the dominant UNIX-like OS used as a server currently. Whether it's more advanced is a different discussion (in my opinion it is in most if not all areas) but it certainly is more popular and is indeed used to provide huge web services such as Google, Amazon, Wikipedia, Slashdot and so on. Not only that but it's also used in supercomputer clusters, scales up to something like 128 processors and is even making headway into the embedded market. Most of the corporate adoption Linux has also is on the server market. It's really not right to say that BSD is the server and Linux the desktop when GNU/Linux is still just making the first few steps thru the entrance to the desktop market.

I understsand that this is a highly contentous issue and I don't mean to press one way or another.  One thing I have learned to appreciate about the BSDs, although its full of *ssh*les, is the care that goes into creating any BSD variant.  They seem to take an integral approach, which some argue is what apple does with their OSes (and hardware).  Whereas linux development seems half-hazard at times, with patches that are forgotten to be thrown in to a default kernel for year old hardware, etc (see ubuntu sleep issue on ibooks with Radeon 9200).  At least one ppc develoepr doesn't have a apple computer, etc.  I can describe this in detail but it is OT.  One argue that the decentralized devloping that gnu-linx undergoes pushes it forward technologically.  Fine, I don't wish to argue this.  (I realize that obsd and netbsd apple laptop support is no-existent and thus one could argue that their centralized devloping is not better.  Sure.  But it's an interesting model, although wasteful due to sometimes reinventing the wheel.  Some people argue that efforts are are often doubled in multiple BSDs.

As for linux as servers, I don't want to get into a pissing match, but see 
[http://utenti.gufi.org/~vic/slides/foil03.html](http://utenti.gufi.org/~vic/slides/foil03.html)
[http://63.249.85.132/fbsd_intro.html](http://63.249.85.132/fbsd_intro.html)

I don't like their lisence either., and I think OBSD is a lot of hype.  Consider that they claim have audited software yet I remember OS X having a problem where encrypted passwords were passed clear using their creation called open ssh, obsd also makes a big stink about auditing their OS but upon closer inspection you find out that this only applies to the core OS and they warn that apps have not been audited. Upon reading this I remember a sense of doom as if by installing apps I would comprimise my install.  Others have criticized obsd by saying something like, 'It's easy to remain secure when you run dated software whose security issues have largely been found and dealt with'.  Now is obsd the most secure OS out there?  Maybe, maybe not if by installing apps you compromises your system.  Yes I know it is usually not used as an OS but as a firewall.  Have I redeemed my self as an gnu-linux user? :)  In any case I do think Hurd will take care of any security issues left.

That's what I said when I wrote about 'converging'.  One thing I want to make clear, 'Just because linux is taking their baby steps towards a desktop does not mean they do not pose a foreseeable threat'.  Consider that business thrives on seeing things down the road.  Genius is seeing things while ther're in the process of germination, or prior (a bastardized chinese proverb).  It's too late one things have manifested, especially for large companies because they can't react and adapt quickly.  Thus, I don't put it past apple to NOT do something about gn-linux-ppc right now, and am convinced that their unfriendiless towards  gnu-linux-ppc is purposeful.

### Post by Ptero-4 on 2005-05-01
[QUOTE=philcolbourn]macs and installing linux on them was new to me. I went for a dual boot, and I am still working out what is best for me.

I have a new powerbook g4 (2005 version) and the 80211g card is not supported (not many are on any platform I think). The touchpad also does not work. I need to get a usb (or perhaps bluetooth) mouse to be able to use the thing.

Osx is good, but a little more buggy than I had expected. eg. the disk utility crashes, will not copy an iso and will not burn a ubuntu iso! Another iso is ok though.

I don't know if bluetooth is supported under linux yet either.

My recommendation is to get your data onto a cd, hold 'c' and boot the osx cd, before you start installing, run the disk utility and create your osx and ubuntu partitions. finish the osx install and then boot abain with the ubuntu install cd and follow their instructions.

i'd practice at least one more time before you put your data back. I think i'll be doing it a few times before i'm happy. also, when you install osx, do a custom install and deselect the extra gig's of languages and themes if you don't want them. also, you don't need IE and MS-Office demo's.

I later installed another osx on a partition and now yaboot is bypassed, so i'll have to fix that.[/QUOTE]
 Which OSX do you use? ### Post by chascon on 2005-05-01
> --OS X security--
I already agreed that with reviewed packages Linux is more secure in that way, but when you do only install OS X stuff you know is reliable both systems do not differ what security goes. Building and installing things from source is also a security risk for Linux; sudo make install.
You haven't thought about the relationship dynamics that propietary software puts onto its users.  It is what sociologist call a empowerment/disempowerment relationbship.  Binary only customers ask the customer trust them, which opens them up to security issues happening under neath their noses, but the binaries companies do not trust their customers to have the source and verify for themselves security issues and improve on source so that they can better meet their needs.  Thus, this relationship puts the user at at least two/three disadvantages AFAIK,
1) It asks that you blindly trust binary software with security for which you can not verify.
2) It creates a dependancy for the customer for meeting feature desire and needs.   
3) all the above put the user at the whim of the software developer and /or market inertia.
Thus, propietary software desempowers the user while disproportiantely empowers the propietary software developer.  Thus, propietary developers ask you to to trust them on blind faith that they are not going to do anything malicious with your info, yet they do not trust you with the source.  This is not the type of ideal relationship that one wants to find oneself in any place in life, let alone with one's computer with bank info, etc.  Sorry, but I don't buy Pauline theology.
Furthermore, having the choice between one propietrary dev and another is not freedom because your choices are still restricted with either selection.

You mention that apple decided that beige G3 were not fast enough to run Panther and therefore disallowed install onto these machines.  This only confirms my illustration of the power relationship dynamics written above, and my point for bringing it up in the first place.  Apple took away from Beige G3s owners the descision whether they wanted to run Panther on G3s, as they did they did with isight/ichat.  The ichat/isight issue was revealing because, as far as I recall, apple put out isight/ichat beta that ran on G3s as well as G4s.  Well when the beta's time was up and they didn't run anymore, they came out with a payware version, else you had buy a new OS X  (Panther) and/or a new hardware (G4s) because they disabled webcam function on the G3s.  They did this despite that they did the betas on G3s!  In addition they locked people into one of he most expensive video cams I knwo about.  Don't say it was apple keeping quality control.  If it was such a quality control issue why did they allow G3s to run the betas in the first place?  If quiality control was such as issue they might have restricted the trial runs to G4s.  Judging from my reading back then, many users were happy the quality they got with their G3s.  Yet, apple took this desicion away from them.  Does this sound benign? Well, I guess 1 million chinese can't be wrong about rice right?  On the risk of sounding like a fundamentalist, my standards are not defined by what is fashionable or necessarily by what others say.  I hope people don't base their morals on what's in.

There are so much examples of this already happening in the past that I can imagine Apple is careful about releasing the specs of the hardware that makes Apple unique. 
Sorry, I don't see this as a problem (fundamentally, other than they are breaking some laws) as I don't believe in patents or copyrights.  Furthermore, I think copyrights and patents are anti-capitalist.  I see them as monopolies.  They are attempts to courner the market afterall.  Capitalism is supposed to allow healthy competition between sellers so that they can offer the best product at the best price and benefit the consumer.  I don't think patents and copyrights do this, because a manufacturer has liberty to charge an unfair price if they are the only ones that are allowed to sell such and such product.  Thus, the value of the product, which is supposed to define selling price, is artificially inflated.  Besides charging a 'fair price' is a capitalist basic.  Read Webber for more precision on the rise of capitalism.
These patents don't allow for enough competitive develpoment within said techonology.  

Lets take this issue of 'intellectual rights' (a fuzzy category), do you think Shakespeare and other masterpieces would exist if this concept existed in older times?  Considering that many works attributed to Shakespeare have been proven to writen by others (called 'plagerism'), and that many of his story lines were rips offs (read Spanish literature from their Golden Age), would we have these works today --that are appreciated by so many-- if the concept of intellectual rights existed then?  No.  Are we better for not having thess restrictions on our freedoms?  I think your literary teachers and professors would argue, YES!  Science and the Arts thrive on the exchange of ideas, in fact science started to develop into its contemporary 'empiricist' manifestation not until scientist started to share and pool their knowledge.  Please don't use the argument that the USA is more techonological advanced because of 'intelletual rights' because they were more technologically advanced than the rest of the world prior to 'intellectual rights'.

We also wouldn't have Elvis Presley because what he did was take a sub-cultural milieau and made it palatable for popular release.  Heck, we wouldn't have rock n' roll at all, because it was based on blues that could have been patented prior to the creation of rock n' roll.  Gee I think patents are clearly a serious restriction on not only personal freedoms but scientific and cultural advancement.  Take Elvis for what it is, hate his music or not, but some would argue you wouldn't have your 3 chord punk or other back to musical fundamentals youth music if it weren't for people such as Elvis for 'stealing' the 'intellectual creations' of others.   Instead of Presley, you can also use Buddy Guy, etc.  We would not even have musical genres (in any contemporary sense) because each genre could be patented itself.  Every song would have to be a style itself.  There would not be any extensive development into the potentialities of each style because any development would be restricted to the artist or band that first created/registered and was granted a patent for the style.  Hell, they might get bored an not properly explore a style leaving a demand for this commodity that could not freely be explored.  Of yes, they might license a style/genre, but this is restrictive and I doubt starving muscians would be willing to pay.  I can't quite recall a saying that used ot be psased among musicians.  It went something like this, 'The best chops (or licks) are those that are borrowed (stolen?)'.

If this all sounds rediculous its becaue it is.  Patents and copyrights put rediculous restriction on society.

### Post by Chunga on 2005-05-02
I'm sorry, but you're contradicting yourself all over the place.  There is one freedom that the GPL explicitly denies.  Yes, it's our favorite part of the GPL.  You can't take GPL'd code and change it without releasing it with the same license.  It's actually stricter than that; you can't even use GPL code (whether it's altered or not) without GPL'ing your entire project.  This restriction is obviously not part of the public domain, and yet the public domain is what you are asking for when you say you are against copyright and patents.

I assume you are against corporations taking GPL code and not giving back to the community?  Then you certainly aren't against copyright because that's what makes the GPL work.  Everyone is aware, on the other hand, how patents are abused; patent reform would be the best thing we could hope for.

And thanks for the lecture on what the GPL is, even though I never doubted the... 'pragmatic quality' of it.

[QUOTE=Len]
Well, there you got me. I seriously thought most of Safari was opensourced and given back. Sure, what Apple does now is perfectly legal considering the LGPL (so actually the KHTML devs made a mistake in licencing if they didn't meant that), but I thought they gave back more as just improvements to the existing source.
BSD is another story. They have chosen for a real free system, not a restrictive GPL, so they should expect it to be used in commercial things, I guess Apple even does not have to give back changes to the source according to that BSD licence. They still do.
[/QUOTE]

Yeah, it's perfectly legal. Doing only the bare minimum to make it legal isn't playing very nice though. There was a great quote on slashdot about this: "The GPL, however much RMS would like it to, doesn't mandate having a social conscience."
There is no license that I know of preventing this sort of "compliance".

The BSD license is not more or less free than the GPL to me. Let me explain why:

The GPL is designed to always retain a set of rights for all the users of a piece of code. The lack of freedom you're talking about is really the lack of an ability to impose restrictions on others which I personally don't consider much of a freedom. The ability to subvert others isn't part of the generally accepted definition for freedom either. Freedom never means you can do *anything* you desire. More extreme examples from the real world is that you aren't allowed to freely go about murdering people in what is considered a free society.

So apart from the copyleft difference that ensures the users of a software package will always retain the freedoms that are stated to be required in the [free software definition](http://www.fsf.org/licensing/essays/free-sw.html), both are as good in my opinion. 

[QUOTE=Chunga]
There is one freedom that the GPL explicitly denies. Yes, it's our favorite part of the GPL. You can't take GPL'd code and change it without releasing it with the same license. It's actually stricter than that; you can't even use GPL code (whether it's altered or not) without GPL'ing your entire project. This restriction is obviously not part of the public domain, and yet the public domain is what you are asking for when you say you are against copyright and patents.[/QUOTE]

Actually it's an interesting question with no simple answer whether abolishing the copyright law would make the GPL unnecessary.

It would mean that controlling the redistribution of a piece of software would become impossible - one of the key freedoms the GPL grants (freedom 2). It would also automatically mean getting freedom 0, the freedom to run a program for any purpose.

Now, the remaining freedoms requiring access to the source code are more difficult. We know that if anyone would ever get their hands on the code they would be free to redistribute it if they desired. I'm not completely clear on the issue so I don't know if you could keep the source private. For a single developer it would be possible of course so I guess some legislation might be required for a GPL-type world. That, or goodwill from people.

So what the FSF might want is that copyright law was abolished AND there would be some sort a law dictating that source code and binaries are not separable and releasing one to the public would require the other.

### Post by Chunga on 2005-05-02
Hi.  I'm not saying that copyright and the GPL are the same thing.  What I mean is that without the existence of copyright, everything would automatically be in the public domain.  Therefore you can't take the code and slap a license (the GPL) on it.  Therefore all of that code has no protection against being used by corporations without them giving back to the community, which is the great thing about the GPL.

I'm not putting down the GPL at all; it was a great idea.  It's definitely needed.  I will say that I think the fact that you can't use GPL code in your project without GPL'ing the whole thing is too restricting, but that's what the LGPL addresses so it's a bit of a non-issue.


All that said, I think open source is the ideal, but I don't find "proprietary software" immoral.  I think a lot of the time companies cut off their noses by not freeing up certain code (airport extreme drivers) and I think hardware companies should always release the specs of their hardware so that we can at the very least write our own implementations, but I'm all for *choices* and if a company chooses to keep their code closed, then I don't look down at that.

Ideas and methods should be free (which is why the current patent system sucks), but I think code is personal, like artwork.  If I'm an artist and I do a great painting, maybe I don't want it be available for everyone and their dog to print.  Same with a person's code.  It's probably a little selfish, but people have always had personal belongings and I don't find it immoral.

But the code is just the implementation of an idea anyway.  If we had major patent reform (so that ideas and methods are truly free), and if hardware companies always released their specs, then we would be completely free to write the implementation ourselves, if there was no Free code available.  Everybody wins.  The company gets to retain their implementation (for whatever reason) and anyone else can also write their own implementation if needed.

Therefore companies that actually do release their code get good karma by the open source people, but there are no arbitrary restrictions placed on them to release any code, and everyone else is free to create other implementations.

Okay I'm done typing.

I know there are many people (nobody in particular) out there who believe closed-source software is immoral and that anybody who writes code somehow owes it to "the community" to give them their code, and that's fine too.  I just don't happen to think that way.

---

### Post by slux on 2005-05-03
Although this thread has pretty much come to it's end I'd like to comment a bit. 

Whether the notion of ownership of property is a natural human way of looking at things or not is completely irrelevant when we're talking about computer programs and really demonstrates that the speaker has been confused by the "intellectual property" advocates. A computer program is not tangible so it is in no way comparable to an ownable physical object.

The only reason an author has any right to impose restrictions on redistribution of his work is the existence of copyright law and that is a relatively new invention (19th century I think, but absolutely not earlier than the 18th). This has nothing to do with property ownership and was originally invented so that the costs of running  printing press could be covered by the owners and to create an incentive for authors to create more. This is not  "giving more choices", it's an arbitrary restriction on everyone except the author and certainly not the "natural state of things".

 Chunga (also) said that a computer program is "an implementation of an idea". I'd say it is nothing more than the idea itself expressed in a strict notation that happens to be interpretable by a computer, just like a mathematical proof. You can write an essay about those ideas in a somewhat less formal way and it is interpretable by a human but that isn't "an implementation" of them either.

---

### Post by Len on 2005-05-03
[color=blue]Again, and to everyone: please keep the discussion clean![/color]
(unless you like it to escalate and being locked)

Wow, I can't leave for a day or there are numerous new posts already ;-).

*-- Patents and copyright, keep, dump or rethink them? --*
I do think if these vanish, hell will break loose. You can keep copyright and drop software patents though.
Imagine, I work 2 years on a nice piece of hardware. I have to earn back the costs I made and make money. Now what would happen if I can't protect it? Other people copy your 2 years of work in seconds and you never get back the costs you made, let alone earning money. You don't care if they improve your invention, you invented it in the first place so it is yours. This is where copyright and patents are for. If they wouldn't exist stealing would be legal, and companies would be crazy if they put money into research.
Dumping copyrights and patents would not automatically dump monopolies. What would happen if someone invents a nice new speaker without patenting it? Indeed, Bose or some other gigant rips it, markets it and makes money of it. Same with software. Big companies will dominate until only one is left, without patents and copyright which are the only thing that protect small companies from stealing.

[i]-- GPL restrictions --[i]
Since GPL software may only be redistributed with other GPL software it is restricted and not compatible with the LGPL or other 'free' licences. If you have code you have no licence on at all then still you may not combine it with GPL stuff. This prevents a lot of things, non-free software where pieces are opensourced cannot be used in the GPL nor can be GPL themselves. The GPL protect itself, but also kills anything that is different. People still have to invent the wheel multiple times, so you can imagine I'm more happy with the LGPL or BSD licence which allow much more, including bundling with closed source and all other kind of software as long as you give your changes to the opensource software back.

*-- Apple dropping support for older hardware --*
Where is a beta test for? Indeed, wiping out the bugs and thouroughfully testing the software. If Apple decides iChat and iSight work better on their G4, let them do that. It is NOT your RIGHT to GET this software, it is THEIR right to provide the software for whatever they like.
If you want a webcam on that machine that badly use another brand. If I decide to release my newest release of my software only for Windows while I have supported Mac OS in the past no one has the right to demand a Mac OS or Linux version like it is their right. They could request it with some nice arguments though.
When you bought your G3 you bought it for the software that was available at that very moment. Why would the machine after 6 years of support suddenly be worthless? Does the old software not run on it anymore? You even have the alternative for another OS, Linux, if you do not like to use Mac OS 10.2 or any other software that is not at the 'latest' version available. I still use System 7.6 with my 5500. It hasn't become less awesome, Illustrator 7 is lightning fast and all thousends of other apps I have on it also still are. They are matured until the next version release. That OS X wouldn't run on it might be a pity, but that's not where I purchased the machine for 8 years ago. The 5 years of support are enough a bonus.
Next time I'm here I hear complains about three dimensional holographic displays not working on the G4 and how bad that support of Apple is because of that... Think about it ;-).

*-- Linus and Bitkeeper --*
As far as I knew any CVS client was fully compatible with it, so no forced Windows use (thank god) by Linus. I don't know enough about them to note any differences in functionality. That metadata for example, did the CVS server provide that as well? As far as I read up the arguments on the kernel mailing list I haven't seen a single thread about that. Only thing that was in discussion was that it was not opensource, and that's why I mentioned it.
Here are the articles I got my info from:
[http://kerneltrap.org/node/901](http://kerneltrap.org/node/901)
[http://kerneltrap.org/node/4968](http://kerneltrap.org/node/4968)


*-- OpenSource vs ClosedSource security --*
Saying that source code can be verified and a binary can not is only true on a certain level. You can exactly monitor what a binary is doing when executed. It it gathering data? Is it making a network connection? Which libraries does it invoke? In other words, a binary can be verified for spyware as well, and that's exactly why there is no spyware distributed at [http://www.macupdate.com](http://www.macupdate.com) or [http://www.apple.com/downloads/macosx/](http://www.apple.com/downloads/macosx/) .
I haven't seen any user verifying source code, so basically it is the same. Linux people verify some packages and hit alarm when they find strange code, warning people not to use that code, and Mac sites remove applications that do unusual things. Mac news sites would immediately hit alarm if a spyware app is distributed. Remember someone spreading a 16KB AppleScript named "Microsoft Office 2004 full installer" on a filesharing network that would ask the user for his password and delete his homefolder (an .sh script can do the same before someone commenting that this is a 'security threat'). The whole mac community was upset, I had a great laugh about that. Even virusscanner companies talked about the first "trojan for Mac OS X" hoping that they could finally sell their useless virusscanners for OS X (there is NO virus for OS X), I actually couldn't stop laughing. So yeah, imagine what happens if a binary saves a file and attemts to send it somewhere. Immediate alarm and panic, thus no threat anymore ;-).

*-- Airport and other hardware specs --*
Since the available wireless GPL drivers do not work with Airport you may conclude that they have made considerable changes to their devices, so "exactly the same as" is not correct. If they decide to keep it secret, fine for them, and I still stand with my first point about protection. I do not think this is a non-issue just because there are other devices using that 'chip'. There is more as that chip on the print, and a whole software part that could do significant things. Giving that free *could* mean giving their research for grab to other companies. Something no one would do with the products that provide their main income. What the difference is remains a guess only Apple could answer. You are allowed to use a wireless PCMIA or USB adapter with your Linux powerbook though, and those are fully compatible with the new Airport Express and Extreme basestations.

Now I hope again I didn't miss something. Weather is nice, I'm out fishing again :P.

EDIT: A nice thing is in development and just released: MoM, a port of MoL for Mac OS X. This will allow you to run OS9, BSD, Linux or whatever on top of Mac OS X without slowdown. Currently not very feature rich, but nice in the future for a real non-emulated experience of another OS. Qemu is a bit slow to try Ubuntu Linux.

EDIT 2: What's wrong with the {i] [/i} tags? I could swear I put them in correctly, yet they show up in the text where they aren't even placed...

### Post by Chunga on 2005-05-03
[QUOTE=slux]I'd say it is nothing more than the idea itself expressed in a strict notation that happens to be interpretable by a computer, just like a mathematical proof. You can write an essay about those ideas in a somewhat less formal way and it is interpretable by a human but that isn't "an implementation" of them either.[/QUOTE]
I'd say code is more than just the idea itself.  It takes work to write code and the writer should be able to release it under whatever terms he wants.  It's not the same thing as the "idea," which should be freely available, but are currently being held up with patents.  Do you also think authors should have to release all their works in the public domain?

Copyrights have a very real and important purpose.  I don't know how many times I have to say this, but **copyrights are the only way the GPL works**!  People need to have the ability to release their stuff under their own conditions.  Without copyright, everything goes to the public domain and is up for grabs to be stolen by corporations.  I'd say that's a good enough reason to keep copyright right there.  I'm surprised so many Linux users here aren't supporting the GPL.  Do you want everything to end up in the public domain?  That's all I'm saying and that's all I've been saying.  I guess by now the answer is that you do, but I don't know why any Linux user would want that.

### Post by slux on 2005-05-04
[QUOTE=Chunga]I'd say code is more than just the idea itself.  It takes work to write code and the writer should be able to release it under whatever terms he wants.  It's not the same thing as the "idea," which should be freely available, but are currently being held up with patents.  Do you also think authors should have to release all their works in the public domain?[/QUOTE]

We're talking about computer programs here. It's a completely separate issue whether copyright is a good system for something else so please keep on topic. Ideas have to be given a form that can be passed around among humans and archived. Algebra is one such (strict) notation. Programming languages and even natural languages are other examples. That's my argument. Would you mind giving some reasons for yours? I acknowledge that every time an idea is given a representation in any form (even inside a person's head) there is some "creative input" from that person into the specific representation he comes up with. That doesn't mean he should have copyright protection on it though.

> 
Copyrights have a very real and important purpose.  I don't know how many times I have to say this, but **copyrights are the only way the GPL works**!  People need to have the ability to release their stuff under their own conditions.  Without copyright, everything goes to the public domain and is up for grabs to be stolen by corporations.  I'd say that's a good enough reason to keep copyright right there.  I'm surprised so many Linux users here aren't supporting the GPL.  Do you want everything to end up in the public domain?  That's all I'm saying and that's all I've been saying.  I guess by now the answer is that you do, but I don't know why any Linux user would want that.


Did you read my post on this at all? I gave the lowdown on what a copyrightless world would probably look like and suggested what would be needed to be done for creating a GPL-like situation thru legislation. I am pretty sure something like this is what the FSF would want happening. The GPL is just a 'clever hack' of copyright. It makes it do things that it was never originally intended for. Furhermore the GPL is just one tool. One can believe in free software and wish for a world without copyright even if it would happen to spell doom for the GPL.

---

[QUOTE=chascon]This is not the same, these mac sites do not perform peer-review.  Peer-review happens at the source level. This is not going on at this level at these sites. ### Post by slux on 2005-05-07
[QUOTE=Len]
You make your own copyright and patents. If you choose to patent a little modification to some other patented object, it's your choice. Copyrights and patents can be crystal clear however, otherwise Linux or OpenOffice wouldn't even exist anymore. That family member is doing well, several companies are bidding for that patent (they wouldn't if they could steal it like you say), and he is offered a job as well for further improvement of his product.
[/QUOTE]

You obviously need to look around a bit. There has been an estimate that Linux is currently infringing on a little under 300 different software patents and Linus's stand on the matter is that no programmer should look at any software patents. I won't talk about patents in general but software patents are very bad thing and most of them are granted for obvious things. I urge you to familiarize yourself with the subject a bit at a website such as [this](http://swpat.ffii.org/patents/index.en.html) 

> Ideology is nice, but I have a computer to work on. Can't finish my job with ideology alone. I know people that are forced to use Windows for several applications. Whether you like it or not, they're not able to finish their work without it. They could consider another job, or programming something themselves which would take a lot of time and they probably want to 'copyright' to earn back their time investments.

I respect people who don't forget their principles whenever it would be inconvenient for them to abide by them.

It's a very ominous proposal that practicality should always be number one and moral/ethics come second. That's where the Enrons and Microsofts of this world are coming from I guess.

> 
Actually the crude X11 OpenOffice port launches faster here on OS X as it does in Ubuntu. I wonder why. Peer improvements are needed for opensource software since this is all developed in free time.

Heh. On my 300MHz iBook G3 the X11 port is unusably slow and unbeliveably ugly. NeoOffice/J is somewhat more responsive and looks a lot better but it is still very sluggish. They both take a very long to start. On Ubuntu, Ooo works very well. Apple's terminal and iTerm are also inconviently slow and OS X tends to use a lot of swap space. It also wastes some space on this tiny 3GB hd so I can't have all the software I had on Ubuntu. And no, it's not trivial to remove everything that's excess.

I have a few other issues but generally OS X manages to be a pretty good OS for this machine. Most functionality works well and fairly quick. In some respects it's better than Ubuntu. I have no doubt that I'll be returning to it after using OS X for a while though.

Most of the major free/open source software aren't developed by volunteers working on their free time by the way. Most of the work going on with Ooo has actually been done by Sun employees and it's originally a proprietary product anyway (it was freed after StarOffice 5.2).

I wanted to add that if closed source was such a great devlopment model (or better as you suggest in some ways by comparing OO with propietary word processors) then longhorn would of come out years ago, and apple (who benefit from darwin's BSD heritage) would not have put out what 4 or 5 releases by the time longhorn finally is expected to come out.  Consider that longhorn has had to drop a lot of features to make their release dates, which keep on extending.  Yes OS X had a head start with the bsd heritage of darwin, but should not a superior dev model be able to compensate for this?  After all you point out that OO is sluggish compared to closed counterparts.   

I might as well point out that in an earlier posting I meant to say 'gnu-darwin (not open darwin) exists because darwin is not free enough'.

It runs system 6 and 7, both systems take only a fraction of RAM (1 MB or below), yet provide a more complete GUI as Gnome. I have put an 1 GB disk in it (40 MB wasn't that much) and dragged hundreds of applications on it. At least they knew how to keep things clean, small and fast those days. Anyway, the machine boots within 10 seconds on OS 7, and almost instantly on 6. It has 8 bit 22 KHz stereo audio (multiple channels), can network with my new Macs, has a web browser (slow with pictures though on that 16 MHz processor), has MS Office 4 (the last good version) with Word, Excel and Powerpoint, has a Midi/Mod editing app with keyboard support, Quicktime, a lot of games, some 3D, Illustrator 3, CAD apps, vector graphic apps, email, ftp/http servers... You name it, it has it. Even the GUI is easily customizable, just copy and paste an icon on a folder or file, give your documents color labels or drag anything to the desktop (OS 9 and before had a desktop folder on each disk so it didn't copy things from an external drive). And it runs it FAST and all that goodness on 8 MB of RAM. Full GUI, before Mac OS X, the OS NEVER had a command line. Sure, it is black and white, but since all graphic cards that provide color are collectors items I will never add one. I can expand it to 128 MB of RAM by the way.
So yeah, you saying that it is 'unethical' to drop iChat USB cam support because it is  possible, well, I think it is unethical that my 16 MHz SE/30 is abandoned. It is able to do almost any office job. Multimedia is a problem, but apart from that, almost nothing. I haven't seen a single Linux GUI system for the 680x0 series though. The only unix you can get on it is Apple's A/UX which was supposed to replace the ancient system before the PPC came. Money shortage and an internal mess never made it happen though. It took years before OS X replaced the ancient system. A/UX is unix with a nice GUI that takes only 2 MB of RAM, yet can do a hell lot of things.
Anyway, if you realized what is possible you would think that Linux is also very, VERY limited in making use of machines. Just like OS X and Windows X(!)P.

Specs can be biased as well. If you use Darwin without DMA and Linux with it is clear that linux will be much faster on disk access. Same goes for network and other things.
The specifications also does not say how well applications or a desktop will perform. Here Mac OS X is a very clear winner as well.
BSD is also a clear winner with GUI apps at least; with linux binary compatibility it launches binaries even faster as on linux itself. And that are partially emulated instructions, not even a BSD build. OpenOffice is a nice one to test.

My SE does with 1, my 5500 with 12.
I think it is very strange that there is no opensource system like that; I mean, commercial systems move on, have to be good looking, have to have everything installed or so, etc, etc. GUInix also strives for this which really shouldn't be the only project. It should aim for simplicity and soberty, yet highly userfriendly customizability. Only system where I saw that was OS 9 and before. If someone could explain me why the opensource community isn't able to write something like that and make an old computer user happy while being superior to proprietary alternatives I would be very grateful. ### Post by chascon on 2005-05-13
>  As far as extension conflicts, well, you can turn them on or off with a single click. 
Pain in the butt.  I've spent literally hours trying to figure out what was conflicting overtime.
I'm sure you're familar with the procedure so why do you make me write it out?  Just out of antagonism?  Well for you people that never used classic.  You go in an turn one extension off. Then reboot.  If that doesn't work, turn another extention off and then reboot again.  If there are 30 extensions, you'll have to do this 30 times.  This may sound simply, althuogh tedious and time consumming, and it can be moreso because problems may arise from combinations of extensions.  So you may also have to test combinations. Let me write it again. Pain in the ass.

> Journalizing didn't exist, protected memory also didn't.

That's a good question. And the explanation that there would be some special "optimizations" for G4 creating the difference is mostly amusing.

[QUOTE=slux]And as for your "benchmarks", I'd really like to see some real numbers and a little more info on what apps you used and how because I've never found any BSD to be faster than Linux (I'm not saying slower either although I do hate FreeBSD's proactive memory freeing tendency) and OS X doesn't seem to do it for me either. And the explanation that there would be some special "optimizations" for G4 creating the difference is mostly amusing.[/QUOTE]

I'm crazy that I do this for you guys, but yeah, here are some benchmarks I just did on my G4 I haven't BSD and linux installed on the same machine right now so I can't compare those. Both on the same disk (although different partitions) and cold unless otherwise noted. Notice that the copying in Linux has to be done only once a boot, after that the files are cached in RAM and will provide biased results:

Duplicating a 257.2 MB mpeg file (Mac = HFS+, Linux + Ext3):
OS X - 12 seconds (duplicate command)
Linux - 21 seconds (cp /test.mp4 /test2.mp4)

Duplicating a folder with 1300 files (46 MB):
OS X - 5 seconds (duplicate command)
Linux - 5 seconds (cp -r /test /test2)

Boot time (Mac OS X uses some trick by prelinking everything or so, nevertheless, why doesn't Linux? It could use a boost you know...)
OS X Tiger - 20 seconds until the desktop fully appears
Linux - 51 seconds until GDM appears, another 15 before Gnome started.
Disabling NTP time synchronizing will substract 10 seconds (although OS X also synchronized)

OpenOffice launch time:
OS X (1.1.2 X11) - 12 seconds until the menu appeared (./soffice)
OS X (warm, close and open) - 2 seconds
Ubuntu (1.1.3 Gnome) - 13 seconds until the menu appeared (./soffice)
Ubuntu (warm, close and open) - 5 seconds
OS X NeoOfficeJ - 25+ seconds (slow Java port).

Networking: No differences in down or up speed at all; the only thing Linux seems to do worse is the DNS lookup. It can take ages for every site to get the IP from the DNS server. Maybe Linux doesn't like my router or so...

Useful specs:
G4 MDD 1.25 GHz (single processor)
1024 MB of RAM
10 GB Linux Ext3 partition, 512 MB swap partition.
80 GB journalized HFS+ on the mac side of the disk, a lot more on other disks which I didn't use (have to use the same disk for tests, even with the same specs disks can differ greatly).
Hoary cleanly installed and updated. OS X install just two weeks old.

Strange enough the very crude OS X X11 port launches quicker as it does on native Linux with Gnome. Imagine how fast an optimized Aqua port will be. One of the strangest things is that the relaunching goes so much faster on OS X - almost instantly. I though it was Linux that cached applications when the RAM was available, which certainly is in this case (1024 MB).
Try OpenOffice Linux on BSD and you'll notice an even bigger difference. Also try the native OpenOffice BSD version. I tried this on FreeBSD, which is no problem for anti-proprietary people to try since it is even more free as the GPL Linux.

The large file copying was very slow on Linux (Ubuntu hoary), while using the same disk as OS X. This is strange, I didn't expect such a giant difference. I redid the test twice (twice a clean boot to avoid RAM caching) on both OS X and Ubuntu. The huge number of little files seem to go equally fast.

As far as that no other benchmarking will be important; I could compare OpenOffice with Appleworks and Ragtime, Gimp with Photoshop, etc, etc, but that doesn't make much sense. The systems are very different and you'll have to bench it yourself with the system native apps you're going to use. It is clear though that OS X is much faster as Linux, at least here. And then I didn't compare the GUI speeds because of the limited ATI Radeon acceleration of the current Linux drivers (that would be a bit mean, wouldn't it?).

---

### Post by slux on 2005-05-13
> **Len]
Duplicating a 257.2 MB mpeg file (Mac = HFS+, Linux + Ext3):
OS X - 12 seconds (duplicate command)
Linux - 21 seconds (cp /test.mp4 /test2.mp4)
[/QUOTE]

I tried this with a 1GHz G4 iBook with 256MB RAM. I had a very near the same size file, used "time cp bigfile1 bigfile2" on a random file (dd if=/dev/urandom of=bigfile) on both operating systems. After a fresh boot OS X managed 37secs and Fedora Core 3 39secs. Smaller difference but the same winner.

I tried doing a sequential read (time cat bigfile > /dev/null) with the same file in question just for fun. Here ext3 fared better with 20/18 secs for read / reread versus OSX's 25/17. 

[QUOTE=Len]
Duplicating a folder with 1300 files (46 MB):
OS X - 5 seconds (duplicate command)
Linux - 5 seconds (cp -r /test /test2)
[/QUOTE]

Tried with 30000 very small files totaling a bit over 250MB. Linux managed to do it in 7m 52s. OS X crashed both times I tried, must've been an issue with the installation or something...

> 
OpenOffice launch time:
OS X (1.1.2 X11) - 12 seconds until the menu appeared (./soffice)
OS X (warm, close and open) - 2 seconds
Ubuntu (1.1.3 Gnome) - 13 seconds until the menu appeared (./soffice)
Ubuntu (warm, close and open) - 5 seconds
OS X NeoOfficeJ - 25+ seconds (slow Java port).


I tested this as well. The OSX port doesn't open an empty document for me which may make it a little unfair but I tried to account for that by opening one and adding the times. Linux still got left behind with 32s first start and 11s restart compared to OS X's 32s and 7s relaunch with X11 already loaded.

[QUOTE]As far as that no other benchmarking will be important said:**
> 

Yeah, and you could compare those to GNOME Office, KOffice or one of the other office suites but I digress..

[QUOTE]
 It is clear though that OS X is much faster as Linux, at least here. And then I didn't compare the GUI speeds because of the limited ATI Radeon acceleration of the current Linux drivers (that would be a bit mean, wouldn't it?).

What you compared was mainly the filesystem implementation, HFS+ vs. ext3 and even then a very limited and small portion of it (file creation, deletion, different types of reads and writes and so on and so on are different cases and you only did one operation at a time which is a very rare situation in real life ). That's not to say those numbers aren't interesting or accurate in the limited scope they apply to but certainly they don't warrant a that sort of a conclusion. GUI responsiveness is IMO a bit more important than heavily filesystem bound startup times and that's what chascon's benchmark was partly getting at. It's not so much about graphics drivers with desktop apps so I don't see how the comparison would be all that unfair. Both NeoOffice and Openoffice.org are almost unusably slow on my iBook G3 as I've mentioned and I'd take twice the startup time if I could get them to work faster.

Now ext3 is the dominant Linux filesystem although it isn't nearly the only one and is known primarily for reliability instead of speed. ReiserFS, which just as free as ext3 BTW, chascon, SGI's XFS and IBM's JFS would be interesting to compare on Linux too.

Thanks for the numbers, they were interesting and have made me more interested in Linux file system performance. :)

### Post by chascon on 2005-05-15
This might answer the question on the matter of why OS 8 feels much faster on  mac SE.  I don't think it has anything to do with the fact that classic was better designed.  It's simply a matter that mdern OSes support much more, thus they are fatter.  You can strip things down you know, for instance linux installs onto palms.

"Originally the kernel was fairly small, but as the capability of computers grew the number of devices the kernel had to control also grew. Many of these no longer fit into a model based on files; for instance, you can't effectively treat networking as a file. More and more code was included into the kernel to handle these sorts of tasks, and the kernels grew. Modern Unix systems are extremely large, the Linux kernel consists of 33 million lines of code."
[http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L4_microkernel_family](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L4_microkernel_family)

Perhaps linux or bsd on mac SE need a shot in the arm from zealots like Len.  If they can stick linux on floppies and palms, then getting linux to run smooth on a mac SE seems doable.

Maybe people will get better performacnce from a micro-kernel on old machines being that it'll run thiner.

PS-Len you make it sound as if there is something fundamentaly wrong with OSS quality.   In doing so you use A/UX  as an example of  economy and simplicity leading to performance .  You know apple supported OSes are not universally fast.  Mklinux, which was supported by apple, was slow as a turtle.

"Even in the best case MkLinux was 15% slower than the monokernel, whereas L4 was about 5-10% slower. Additional development of certain portions of the Linux system, not carried out for the testing, was likely to improve this to some degree."
[http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L4_microkernel_family](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L4_microkernel_family)
It seems that Mklinux is even slower than linux.

### Post by Len on 2005-05-17
---- OK, now finally on to slux ----

I'm glad at least someone took tests as well :).
There are a few things I want to make clear before the Ext3 filesystem is dumped as being 'slow' compared to similar safe or even safer and more feature rich filesystems:
Linux being slower on copying files doesn't necessarily mean ext3 is inferior to HFS or XFS; DMA and disk drivers make or break speed.
Mac OS 9 included the driver on the disk itself, so 3rd party companies could also write their own drivers. Silverlining for example provided a driver which could improve speed up to 300% on your Mac disk. Still the filesystem was HFS or HFS+. Also, enlarging your software disk cache and other settings can make some change. In other words, if something like ReiserFS or XFS is faster under linux it would be just as likely that the disk driver is better optimized (or has been configured better) as that the filesystem handles things better.
Your test with Fedora already proved that -or- that one has a better configuration -or- the version of OS X you are using is worse as Tiger (or that I'm just doing something very wrong).

About OpenOffice launching a new document, I thought of that:
You can launch openoffice from Linux without making a new document too. I did that by opening the soffice binary from a Gnome terminal. I believe the right path is something like /usr/share/openoffice/program/soffice. I should have mentioned that since it was my intension to keep the tests fair (although they do not say much, Windows is the fastest of all with MS Office that loads at boot yet I don't like the OS even if my Mac was ten times slower). Microsoft Office for the mac is less buggy and also much faster but I like the .sxw file format. Smaller, cleaner, superior. AppleWorks isn't very nice when importing/exporting to MS Word, tables and other things **** up more often as in OpenOffice. I can save everything on the mac as a .PDF though, but people here do not accept it (PDF?? That isn't built in Windows so that isn't a standard, and if I want to download the file you mentioned (acrobat reader) it complains about a virus!).

I'm very curious about the cause of the major difference between my large file under OS X (Tiger) and Linux (Ubuntu), I didn't expect that at all, 15 % was my bet. You could try ext3 or another filesystem with two different systems (BSD and Linux) or drivers to see if you notice differences to prove that the driver/system is the main cause of the slowdowns. I just hope I know enough so I don't miss any setting like disk cache or so when I setup these systems myself. Or I should just compare them 'as provided'.

If I could do other accurate tests I would, but there are about 1000 settings that can influence these raw tests. For a desktop OS the quickness of available applications and features make or break the OS unless the speed is dramatic.
Benchmarks are very interesting however, so if I get the chance I'll test again :).

EDIT: LOL, I had to split up my post:
"The text that you have entered is too long (31710 characters). Please shorten it to 30000 characters long."

### Post by dimoni on 2005-05-18
I just install it las weekend. Everything when find except for minor problems like sound and other things your relative can live on meanwhile.
I had some problems at first. Don't know what caused them but ubuntu failed to install because of one or both of the following reasons:
1. My main partition was HFS+
2. I was trying to install ubuntu after Mac OS X. So as from dev/disk11

In order to solve it, I made an image of my whole mac (you can do it with Netrestore or carbon copy cloner or  with asr command through terminal app).
Then I reformated the entire disk as HFS (so Mac extended not extended-journaled). Allowing two partitions: one for Ubuntu and another one for OS X.
Afterwards I installed Ubuntu on the FIRST place and then I restored the OS X image on the leftover partition.
Notice that in order to restore the image you need at least a basic os x installation so you can run NetRestore (native os x app) to restore it. Therefore, before reformatting my disk I installed a basic os x on an external hard drive.

Probably there are quicker ways to install linux on mac, but this one worked for me and it is save!!

I hope it works for you also if you give it a try.

dimoni

Yes, I know we can't physically do that, but consider that given that OSes get fat with time, there is no reason to assume that classic wouldn't also get fat and not run (or run as slow as alternatives) on the SE too!

### Post by slux on 2005-05-19
> **Len]---- OK, now finally on to slux ----
Linux being slower on copying files doesn't necessarily mean ext3 is inferior to HFS or XFS said:**
> 

I'm reluctant to believe that as the Linux IDE driver is very mature and IDE disks share a common interface across architectures. Then again, I'm no kernel dev so I can't be certain. In any case, ext3 is known to be the old, trusty and compatible but not so fast filesystem.

> 
Also, enlarging your software disk cache and other settings can make some change. In other words, if something like ReiserFS or XFS is faster under linux it would be just as likely that the disk driver is better optimized (or has been configured better) as that the filesystem handles things better.


I can provide hdparm output and similar from both OSes. I checked that had DMA and a fast UDMA mode enabled on Fedora. Not that it wouldn't show as a terrible result if they weren't but some minor tweaking could of course be still possible.

[QUOTE]If I could do other accurate tests I would, but there are about 1000 settings that can influence these raw tests. For a desktop OS the quickness of available applications and features make or break the OS unless the speed is dramatic.
Benchmarks are very interesting however, so if I get the chance I'll test again :).


Well, on this old iBook Ubuntu certainly flies compared to OS X. Many others have said the same. You mentioned somewhere that you don't think the kernel plays a major part in the responsiveness but I can tell you that it does. Pre-emption and low latency can make a huge difference and memory management can work to various degrees. Of course the window drawing etc. does have a role too and there OS X probably does win though.

We could try testing with benchmarking utils or a similar script doing fs operations. mongo, bonnie++, iozone and postmark are examples of filesystem benchmark apps. I think I'm going to try with bonnie++ as it's so widely available (can get it with up2date -i bonnie++ on Fedora).

[LinuxGazette](http://linuxgazette.net/102/piszcz.html) has made a recent benchmark that I found pretty interesting and the ReiserFS site [namesys.com](http://www.namesys.com) has a collection of benchmarks.

### Post by slux on 2005-05-19
> **Len]Yes. I wonder if it is possible to configure the disk drivers themselves too. I remember configuring disk drivers with HardDiskToolkit under the classic Mac OS. You'd be surprised how many things can be configured and the difference they can make. Some SCSI disks could even be configured on-chip instead of with jumpers like disabling hardware read cache, etc. A shitload of settings are possible, and many optimizations are possible for different goals in speed and stability.[/QUOTE]

You can do a lot of this with hdparm, it lets you change many parameters of modern hard disks if they support it.

> 
Even the filesystem itself can be customized, think about allocation block sizes said:**
> 

ext3's default blocksize is 4096 too, I believe. Certainly Fedora created it's volumes with that.

[QUOTE]
As a desktop system I always found it slower. Of course Linux is faster as OS X when loaded without GUI, one of the reasons why I use it as non-desktop system. I doubt application launch time is faster at all though; unless you are using Mac OS 10.1 which was rather slow. I wonder if the Ubuntu kernel is built preemptive by default, but I always build my own kernel anyway (the tests were done on a fresh, unmodified installation though). I think that with lighter windowmanagers you can get better GUI speeds as with OS X, but the light window managers are very meager. I hope that changes and if not I can still consider starting my own sometime.


Well the application launch times are of some interest although I'll take a system as responsive as GNU/Linux over OS X's sluggishness on this machine (and it's 10.3/Panther) any day even if it means slightly longer startup times. I'd like to time firefox and various other apps available for both systems though. The ubuntu is pretty much guaranteed to be pre-empt. You could find the parameters from /proc or /sys if it's compiled with the needed option.

So I don't really agree with you that OS X would have a more responsive GUI overall, but it's pretty hard to measure accurately, taking all the factors into account of course.

[QUOTE]
Bonnie++ looks nice indeed. I wonder if it can be built for Mac OS X and takes advantage of the OS X libs though. No idea if that's important for this.


Well it does standard filesystem operations the way the POSIX standard defines them and as commonly used in the C programming language so that shouldn't be an issue. The freshmeat project page for Bonnie++ had a comment stating that OS X implements at least one of these calls it's using unreliably so it might not be the best option after all though. Nevertheless, it is available on Darwinports for anyone wishing to run on OS X.

IOZone is another benchmark utility that could be used, I even found binaries with a quick google. Standard Bonnie should work fine too.

> 
Wow, that's quite surprising; ext2 and ext3 lose on almost all fronts! Those other filesystems are as safe or even safer as ext3 as far as I've read about them... Didn't expect this since ext3 is certainly the most popular filesystem, and although I knew of XFS that it was faster at some points I didn't expect all filesystems to beat ext3 overall. Unfortunately HFS+ isn't tested with it. Does the popularity of ext3 have a reason?


ReiserFS is still used by a number of major players - SUSE, Gentoo, Linspire, Xandros. It's also offered as an install-time option by many others along with JFS and XFS. ext3's popularity mostly comes from it being the follower of ext2 and despite it's drawbacks being a very solid filesystem. I would, too, like to see more distributions moving away from it. Reiser4 is promising performance so much better than anything else that it might be adopted by some but it still has ways to go before it can be regarded as a proven FS.

> 
By the way, do there exist any good resources for the "UFS" (Unix File System)?
It is supported in OSX but case-sensitive, which I don't like. I could do some tests though to see what performance that one has.


The main disadvantage with UFS is that it lacks journaling like ext2 which means long filesystem checks and a significantly greater chance of breaking. Journaling does slow down some operations but Google reveals a HFS+ vs. UFS test which suggested that UFS is significantly slower. I find case-insensitive filesystems repulsive as opposed to your dislike of sensitive ones. ;)

### Post by Len on 2005-05-20
hdparm... Wow, never took a thorough look at it, I certainly need to keep up. According to the man page it can do quite a few things. OK, it can't edit all modes but those IDE disks don't have those anyway.
[http://www.rt.com/man/hdparm.8.html](http://www.rt.com/man/hdparm.8.html)

About OS X and Ubuntu, measuring isn't necessary if you have the idea you can work faster with one or the other. Ubuntu is preemptive already... well, in that case I really should start a full featured (OS 9 & X alike) light GUI. Unlike some now thinking I am a 'linux hater', I like the system. Having alternatives is always perfect. I just don't like it as a desktop; Gnome is too slow and not much compared to the Mac GUI while IceWM or XFCE have way too less features. 
Now how do I start on that with just a little C++ experience and no single experience of X and all its libs :P. I'm sure Linux, or BSD, can be a rather quick yet full featured GUI system on older machines. OS 7 could do with a few megs of RAM, so *nix should be able to do it with protected memory and multitasking with 32 MB. The Lisa (1983) which came before the macintosh had a full desktop system with protected memory and that machine had 1 MB of RAM. Replacing X will be too hard; that will mean you have to rewrite all device drivers (graphic cards, mouse, etc). It could be minimized though.

IOZone has some benchmarks on their site as well. The reliability is easily tested; just run both and compare the results :).
[http://users.tkk.fi/~mweissen/iozone-macosx.html](http://users.tkk.fi/~mweissen/iozone-macosx.html) has a Mac binary and some benchmarks. He says he hasn't tested it above 10.2 yet has benchmarks of the G5 which all came with 10.3.

That ext3 is the follower of ext2 would be a reason for me not to use it. I painfully remember the days where a single crash meant a disk repair, in many of the cases two or three to repair all damage if at all possible. Trying to find correct settings for X11 was a pain then (on my old macs it crashed beyond control-alt-backspace). Ext3 may be a proven filesystem, but it isn't perfect as well. Maybe an idea for the next release of Ubuntu to try something different, or give the choice? If the differences are as big as showed on those sites it should give a little boost to the boot process and starting apps as well.

Another thing I do not understand (a bit off topic) is that Linux starts all services at boot one after another. Sure, some services rely on others but hotplug, ntpdate and network sure take their time while waiting for hardware. Since Linux is a multitasking system it could also go like this:
Starting network...            [OK]
Synchronizing clock...        [running]
Hotplug...                          [starting]
Starting CUPS...                 [OK]

This would take away a lot of boot time, yet not causing conflicts and since those long delay processes are just waiting they are not eating processor time as well. Maybe a stupid idea, but on the other hand, why wait for starting processes when you have a full multitasking system?

So... USF doesn't even do journalizing... I wonder why they included that one then and not something like XFS or ReiserFS. Since I am a real GUI user (sure, I can do with a command line, but I like a GUI, much quicker and simpler) I don't want filenames to be the same except for the upper or lowercase characters. I sort, don't search. That's why I didn't buy Tiger for Spotlight, which I find pretty useless, and still organize my GMail mailbox. Heck, I even hate file extensions. (off topic) Filetype/creator codes were much handier: a TEXT document by Simpletext would open with simpletext, and a TEXT document by BBEdit would open with BBEdit unless it wasn't available, then Simpletext would open it.
All .txt files open with the same app, even if you created them in different applications. With only 3 characters for a file extension many overlap as well. Luckily OS X still supports the old system: filetype/creator codes inside the header of the file.(/off topic)

Anyway HFS+ it is on the mac, UFS isn't a choice then... Another thing I wonder about Mac OS X is why those "applictation packages" and all other packages (extensions, frameworks, bundles, libs ...) are folders with an extension instead of a suitcase file, or a stored/compressed archive like Java does. This sure would speed up filesystem operations a lot. OS 9 used resource forks or suitcase files for that, causing it to only be about 1000 files or so. OS X and Linux use over 100.000 files which not only makes automatic defragmenting necessary but slow down things a lot (file searching, indexing, copying) and maybe even are a bigger risk for FS errors. I actually hoped for that feature being reimplemented in Mac OS 10.3 already. If OS 9 did this and Java does this, why not the next-gen OSses? It simplifies, organizes and speed up things without thinking of a new FS or disk driver. Combined with a light but full featured GUI that would beat OSX, Windows, Gnome and KDE to death with its simlicity (which also makes many features 'accessible'), speed and compatibility such a system would conquer the world. GPL or not, it would just be better at most fronts and that's what counts ;).

Guess it's a matter of being compatible with classic BSD UNIXes. Linux HFS+ support isn't perfect either so it's nice that UFS is an option.

XFS, JFS and ReiserFS are off-limits for Apple unless they change their license from APSL to GPL (at the same time enabling all GPLed projects such as the Linux kernel to use Apple code)  or persuade (with money or otherwise) IBM, SGI or Namesys to release them with a different license.

> Since I am a real GUI user (sure, I can do with a command line, but I like a GUI, much quicker and simpler) I don't want filenames to be the same except for the upper or lowercase characters. I sort, don't search.

Whether the underlying filesystem is case-sensitive doesn't have anything to do with sorting. A GUI should probably provide sorting case insensitively anyway.

> (off topic) Filetype/creator codes were much handier: a TEXT document by Simpletext would open with simpletext, and a TEXT document by BBEdit would open with BBEdit unless it wasn't available, then Simpletext would open it.
All .txt files open with the same app, even if you created them in different applications. With only 3 characters for a file extension many overlap as well. Luckily OS X still supports the old system: filetype/creator codes inside the header of the file.(/off topic)

Still OS X has been advocating the use of extensions for application authors and supporting something isn't very useful if no-one uses it. Tiger introduced some interesting yet-to-be-used filesystem metadata features so we'll have to wait and see. I agree that the use of three letter extensions is not a very elegant solution. I'm not sure what would be best though. Unix magic numbers is an interesting one in that it doesn't separate the definition into metadata. A file's format is, after all, extremely closely associated with it's content. In any case I'll take metadata any day over extensions.

>  OS X and Linux use over 100.000 files which not only makes automatic defragmenting necessary but slow down things a lot (file searching, indexing, copying) and maybe even are a bigger risk for FS errors. ;).

GNU/Linux doesn't have any kind of a "automatic defragmenting" function though. OS X wouldn't need it nearly as much as it does if it didn't choose to have the paging file on the filesystem instead of a separate swap partition.

### Post by chascon on 2005-05-21
[QUOTE=][/QUOTE]
It's important to note that bsd does have a approch that addresses the same issues that journalling has.  I believe I already wrote it; It's called softupdates.  Thus, one cannot critique bsd for not "even having journalling".  They simply have chosen to take a different approach.  I assume this is debatable but it theoretically lends itself to a faster fs than journalling.  If UFS or FFS is not faster than ext3, it may be for other reasons.

Panther does have an automatic defrag'ing.  It's called hotfile clustering.
You can google info find info on these.

I'd like to see ubuntu use rieser too but reiser 4 is not trusted by many yet untill it is proven, but there's version 3 or what ever it is labelled.

As for the legitimacy of open/closed issue of modern comps, all modern computers (micro and super) use the Von Neumann architecture which falls under the public domain.  So apple does not 'own' their architecture as has been suggested here.  Apple can't own their architecture because it is the Von Neumann arch and thus public.  There is more sense in asking apple to make their own architecture than there is in asking linux-ppc users to make theirs, afterall the Von Neumann arch is public, ours and not apple's. For apple to truely create 'their own' computer arch they need to create something else than a 'stored-program computer', what ever that may be.  Good luck on creating and selling that apple:)

---

[quote=slux]Guess it's a matter of being compatible with classic BSD UNIXes. Linux HFS+ support isn't perfect either so it's nice that UFS is an option.
Yes, if you look at it that way... I just mount the ext3 volumes in OS X anyway now.

> **slux]XFS, JFS and ReiserFS are off-limits for Apple unless they change their license from APSL to GPL (at the same time enabling all GPLed projects such as the Linux kernel to use Apple code) or persuade (with money or otherwise) IBM, SGI or Namesys to release them with a different license.[/quote]
Why? If I remember correctly the GPL wasn't such a 'viral licence' said:**
> Whether the underlying filesystem is case-sensitive doesn't have anything to do with sorting. A GUI should probably provide sorting case insensitively anyway.
Yes, but it would still make a difference, thus meaning you have to keep that sensitivity in mind. If this was not the case I guess case sensitivity was useless.

> **slux]Still OS X has been advocating the use of extensions for application authors and supporting something isn't very useful if no-one uses it. Tiger introduced some interesting yet-to-be-used filesystem metadata features so we'll have to wait and see. I agree that the use of three letter extensions is not a very elegant solution. I'm not sure what would be best though. Unix magic numbers is an interesting one in that it doesn't separate the definition into metadata. A file's format is, after all, extremely closely associated with it's content. In any case I'll take metadata any day over extensions.[/quote]
Indeed, the extensions just plain suck and was one of the reasons of using a Mac. Metadata is something like the 'finder info' was in OS 4-9. It could contain type and creator code, comments and all kind of flags. It is a shame they finally introduced those stupid .xxx extensions.

[quote=slux]GNU/Linux doesn't have any kind of a "automatic defragmenting" function though. OS X wouldn't need it nearly as much as it does if it didn't choose to have the paging file on the filesystem instead of a separate swap partition.[/QUOTE]
Maybe not, but if the applications were packaged it wouldn't matter much at all. I use swapcop to set my OS X swapfiles to my OS 9 volume. That one doesn't fragment at all, yet I modify much. Logical, it only contains about 20 000 files for the system and over one thousand apps, which I already think is much. Nothing compared to Windows, OS X and desktop Linux though said:**
> As for the legitimacy of open/closed issue of modern comps, all modern computers (micro and super) use the Von Neumann architecture which falls under the public domain. So apple does not 'own' their architecture as has been suggested here. Apple can't own their architecture because it is the Von Neumann arch and thus public. There is more sense in asking apple to make their own architecture than there is in asking linux-ppc users to make theirs, afterall the Von Neumann arch is public, ours and not apple's. For apple to truely create 'their own' computer arch they need to create something else than a 'stored-program computer', what ever that may be. Good luck on creating and selling that apple
This is not a point.
What is the point is that Apple doesn't have to reveal all the specs of the hardware being used in their machines; and do not have to support systems or applications other as their own. Airport Extreme is not public, the IBM G5 processor design also isn't, also not the motherboard design.
If it was it would be a kind of strange: I'd make a computer for a certain task and suddenly people all over the world start claiming it and demanding I support their programs instead of vica versa.
The architecture you talk about is not patented or so, so yes, you can still create your own Linux PPC box without having to worry about supporting Mac OS, Windows PPC or any other dubious system. Or you could use an Intel or AMD processor based machine. It is easy to build one of those especially for Linux.

### Post by Len on 2005-05-22
LOL, it was entirely about Airport Extreme support.

Apple has the right to reserve these specs, and if you don't like it you must indeed create a PPC machine yourself; which probably would still be the same architecture by the way.

It only means that this scheme can be used freely by everyone. In other words, you may make your own PPC box as well if IBM doesn't mind fabricating PPCs for you. If they do, you put together an intel machine which also falls under this 'scheme'.

### Post by slux on 2005-05-23
> **Len]
