Yeah, guess that would be nice, but it would need LVM/EVMS used by default for it to be possible and might not be used all that much.

> 
Why? If I remember correctly the GPL wasn't such a 'viral licence' said:**
> 

First of all the whole "viral license" things needs to finally be put to rest. It's a loaded term introduced by a Microsoft leader. Ballmer, was it? at least he referred to GPL as "cancer". It simultaneously has a negative connotation and is patently false. I've yet to see a "viral" license and I don't think it's possible to make one within the bounds of copyright law. No license spreads like a virus. If you don't like the terms someone else's work is provided under, you don't have to use it. Simple, isn't it?

The difference between GNU Nano, a minimal standalone console text editor, and a filesystem is that you have to introduce a filesystem pretty intimately into your kernel code. At the absolute minimum it's unclear whether or not you could mix GPL and APSL like that and I'd say the answer is probably no.

[QUOTE]
Yes, but it would still make a difference, thus meaning you have to keep that sensitivity in mind. If this was not the case I guess case sensitivity was useless.


OK, my dislike of case-insensitivity mostly stems from Windows where the filesystem often fails to preserve the case and you somehow end up with all-caps filenames or just the extension etc. OS X does a better job preserving the case I think.

[QUOTE]It is not entirely true that the linux filesystems do nothing about defragmenting. Like HFS+ ext3 does a smart file placement. If you want to know the difference, install Windows 2000/XP on a FAT or NTFS filesystem and watch the fragmentation: at least 20% already. My HFS+ volumes never reached over 0.3%, also with OS 9. Linux never reported more as '3% non-continuous'. For windows it really doesn't matter much if you place the page and temp files somewhere else, it still fragments within days.


Have you got a reference for this smart placement? I've not heard of it before. GNU/Linux does have an extremely little used defragmenting tool. Th filesystems tend to fragment a bit but only in about a decade or two. Separating partitions beyond swap naturally helps too, I tend to have /usr, /tmp, /home, /var on different partitions space permitting.


> This is not a point.
What is the point is that Apple doesn't have to reveal all the specs of the hardware being used in their machines; and do not have to support systems or applications other as their own. Airport Extreme is not public, the IBM G5 processor design also isn't, also not the motherboard design.


You're mistaken about the G5, just look at [IBM's reference documentation](http://www-306.ibm.com/chips/techlib/techlib.nsf/products/PowerPC_970_and_970FX_Microprocessors).

> 
If it was it would be a kind of strange: I'd make a computer for a certain task and suddenly people all over the world start claiming it and demanding I support their programs instead of vica versa.

When I buy a piece of hardware I expect to be able to use it. It's not even asking for complete schemas of the device, just a "user's manual" similar to what you can find for the G5 (970fx) from IBM. No-one has talked anything about supporting any programs. Who are you to say what task I use my computer for. You may have manufactured it and sold it to me but after that point it's *my property* and I'm free to do whatever I want to with it.

This isn't limited to just Apple. Hardware manufacturers should provide documentation on how their hardware can be used and not just a crummy w32 driver I may not be able to use now or at some point in the future. That's how it used to be back when there was no need for a free software movement.

And it doesn't matter the slightest bit whether or not Apple "has" to do this. Obviously they don't have to anyway. This is about what's right.

### Post by slux on 2005-05-24
> **Len]Why LVM? The 'disk manager' just creates another partition, makes the necessary folders and add them to the PATHs  said:**
> 

Just because creating two separate structures wouldn't be a very clean solution and debian packages contain absolute paths. It's just better and more simple to do. :)

> 
Yes, I know, these ideas are a bit drastic, but hey, I'm a GUI junkie, thinking a GUI should not only eliminate the command line needs, but also be way more practical. OS 9 did that, OS X tries it although setting up a network share is a pain while apt-get libatalk in debian plus giving a few paths is enough, similar in Ubuntu.


I'm very skeptical on that being possible. The unix philosophy of combining various command line tools in countless ways is difficult to incorporate into a GUI. Automator tries to do something like that but I don't know how well it succeeds.

Both the GUI and the command prompt have their limitations but it's true that all tasks should be doable thru a GUI (even if it will be a lot of work compared to doing it from the command line in some cases). OS 9 didn't have the command line at all and that did limit the user.


[QUOTE]Very simple, but a filesystem doesn't have to be coded into a kernel; OS X uses plugins. Not hard for Apple to make XFS or so a kernel plugin loaded at boot so it is bootable as well. 

I assume you're talking about kexts which are fairly similar to Linux kernel modules. The problem here is that once the module is loaded, it effectively becomes a part of the kernel so my point still stands. It's probably not legal.


> Whoops, typed too quickly I guess. Logical, how else do you want to sell your processor to other manufacturers :P.

How else do you want to sell your 802.11g chipset to other manufacturers? ;)

Actually, you can find similar documentation for the SPARC processor family even though Sun doesn't try to sell it to other manufacturers. It's just meant for anyone wishing to program for the processor. And the WiFi chip is kind of a specialised processor that someone would want to program for too (probably in order to get the device working for him)



> 
That's right, but you knew when you bought it that your airport extreme isn't documented, so you could have made the decision not to buy it. I'd buy it because I don't care, I use it with the Mac OS anyway, you probably want a PCMIA/USB wireless adapter.

Yes, but is it ethical from Apple to refuse to give the specs to me if I ask for them and it doesn't hurt them in any way? (or possibly results in less damage for  them than it does for me)

I doubt you'll find *any* moral theories for which the answer is yes to that question. Certainly not in ones that prevail in western culture. Cooperation and sharing is generally regarded as a good thing.

Now, if it would do some kind of harm to them, this secrecy might be okay. (under some moral systems)

You touched on a few such possible excuses below


> It is also a question of support, those companies do not want 1000's of emails from Mac/Linux users and they want to ensure the reputation of 'it just works' instead of 'it works partially'. ATI and NVidia probably want to keep their I/O secret for each other, and maybe Apple also protects an airport protocol change or so.

I've never heard of any company getting thousands of complaints for a driver not developed by them and it's not that those don't exist. Probably most of the Linux device drivers available today are developed by people outside the companies that manufactured the devices. So unless you have any kind of substance to prove it's a possible scenario I'm reluctant to even consider it as a legitimate worry. What's more, their reputation won't be "it just works", in some cases it will be "it doesn't work at all". I for one don't think more of Apple & Broadcom as a result of their refusal to cooperate.

The rest of what you suggest is so ambigious that it doesn't really need to be considered but I've heard the arguments/excuses that are repeatedly voiced in defense of proprietary 3d accelerator drivers and 802.11g WiFi cards voiced by a random ATI/NVIDIA fan often enough and they don't contain much substance either.


> 
I do not like all that secretive stuff, but I also do not blame those companies for keeping secret what's theirs, or even what they used from others. You have the choice of buying another piece of hardware which is documented.

This is like saying that if someone mistreats you, you always have the option of going to someone who treats you better. It's true but it doesn't make it any more okay for that person to mistreat you.

The Xbox 360 is probably worse than Apple BTW as it will certainly again have all kinds of DRM barriers trying to prevent running unauthorised code on it and they'll learn from their mistakes.. New world Macs have a substantially more open architecture than the older ones that Linux and the BSDs were made to work on after substantial amount of reverse engineering. Back when BeOS was still alive, Apple also prevented Be inc. from porting it to the first G3 macs by not releasing the specs so they've actually gotten a bit more open.

### Post by slux on 2005-05-25
> **Len]In that case a larger drive means a full reinstall then, unless you decide to mount it at the /bin folder or something like that... Or can you add a device to a volume group later on?
[/QUOTE]

Well... yes. One of the major points of using LVM/EVMS is that you can remove and add physical volumes to the group without any downtime. This is especially true for server hardware with hotswap drives, of course.

> 
A GUI should provide different ways of doing things as the command line does. A GUI frondend for 'cp' makes no sence for example. I didn't feel limited under OS 9 and before, not at all said:**
> 

A GUI is very cumbersome for operations you need to repeat n times. Or for ones where you use the output of one program as the input of another. 'cp' does have a frontend, or rather the system call it uses has.

I'll give a couple of examples:

One of the things I do daily at my work is administering over a hundred GNU/Linux desktops. They often need new software installed. Using synaptic and manually selecting the packages, clicking install and closing the application is not a practical solution for this. A command line script iterating over every hostname and issuing apt-get commands over ssh is.

A smaller scale task that one might want to achieve on his personal system could be as simple as converting a set of images that are contained in albums inside a single directory (many subdirectories). I have wanted to do this (or maybe similar things to audio files) many times. Trying to achieve this thru a GUI is either a lot of manual work or involves finding a separate application for the sole task while it's very easy from the bash prompt. You can also add additional requirements for the task at hand and bash will very likely be able to acommodate those too (while a GUI app might have limited functionality and send you searching for another one which in turn might not even exist).


[QUOTE]
Well, you could say the same about plugins for applications. Those however are compatible. Also, ext3 already has been ported to Mac OS X in form of a .kext extension. It is easier to have something under your own licence or a more free licence though because then you can include it into the kernel itself.

Have a look at [http://www.gnu.org/licenses/gpl-faq.html#GPLAndPlugins](http://www.gnu.org/licenses/gpl-faq.html#GPLAndPlugins) .
Plugins could be implemented in many ways while the fork & exec methods can't really be used with a kernel driver module.


[QUOTE]
The airport is more as a chipset alone and not sold to other manufacturers. If it was just a chip, any 802.11g driver would work.


I don't really understand your logic here. Just like a program written for the 970fx won't work on a SPARC,  you need different 802.11g drivers for different chips but if you have one for a given chip, it will work with all cards using the same chip with little or no additional work. For example, the original Airport driver has the same core with other Orinoco cards with some small modifications. 

> 
But how do you know it won't damage them in any way? They do not keep it secret just to make fun of you I assume.

You really have tremendous faith in Apple's good intentions in every matter. Let's just say I see no supporting evidence for it and don't know of a single case where releasing a device driver or just specifications as free software has resulted in any damage being done. On the contrary, it's often resulted in goodwill towards the company and the drivers being improved.

Sure, there's the remote possibility that they have perfectly good reasons for keeping it all secret but until you or anyone else will provide convincing arguments I won't believe that is the case. Indifference (towards the free software / open source community) or downright malice seem like the much more probable motivations.

> 
Ever ported a small piece of software? You'd be surprised what you get on mail. Thousands of questions you, the porter, has nothing to do with, numerous feature requests, flames because of it not working, flames, because you didn't port it to another system... If such a small thing can get your mailbox full everyday I can imagine what happens if someone purchases your device and uses it with another driver.

Since people are today trying to use Airport extreme with GNU/Linux just to be disappointed when it's not working at all, I have no doubt whatever flames Apple would receive (or receive regarding all things GNU/Linux) already crowd their mailbox.

I don't see how releasing the source to their driver for Darwin would change too much at all. Or even less if they only released the specifications.


> So you think they keep stuff secret for no reason at all then? It wouldn't be for Linux; if Linux supported the ATI/NVIDIA cards they would sell more.Airport: same story, Apple would sell more computers, everyone wants a Mac Mini, it is an excellent silent server machine (no noise, 20 watt power usage, no heat and as smaller as your internal optical drive). With Linux Airport support on that thing it would be the 1st choice wireless server, well, for me at least. If I happened to have a wireless network which I don't :P.

The GNU/Linux market on the desktop side is such a small one that it is often overlooked and compared to x86 desktop Linux, GNU/Linux on PPC desktops is just microscopic. ATI/NVIDIA do support Linux - on x86 and in their own limited way that seems to be enough for many (unfortunately).

In the case of Apple they probably don't care about Linux on PPC (which I am currently inclined to believe) or they try to thwart it like they did with BeOS. After all, they still are the vendors of a competing OS for which they just recently released a circa 150€ upgrade.

> 
Wait a second, you are now saying that you are mistreated because you do not get the specs of your purchased device when you knew they weren't available? Am I also mistreated when I buy an XBox 360, find out that ppc Linux doesn't boot on it and I won't get the specs? It is still you who chose to buy the device, the device is also fully yours.


Look at it this way. I am asking (and many others as demonstrated by the petition) Apple to release the driver or the specs to the broadcom 43xx card they market with the name Airport Extreme so that I could use the device with the software I'd like to use.  To date, Apple has failed to do this and I have no reason to believe that they have a good reason for this (and they have not provided a bad one either for that matter). 

Also, if you buy hardware the specs may be a very fair thing to ask for as they are effectively the instructions to use the hardware. A driver working at some point in time on a single operating system that I have no access to beyond that isn't much.


>

### Post by chascon on 2005-05-25
Lets take a game strategy outlook:
Lets say we award 5 points to the consumer if he chooses open hardware because he gains support for his free OS.  Conversely, we have to award him 0 for choosing closed hdware because he looses support and freedom.


choice/decision, d

Selection of choices:	   	
1. run closed hardware which limits freedom, d1 
					=0 loss of freedom		
2. Open which is liberating, d2 
                                   =5 for gain of freedom
				
d1=0 meaning there is a loss of freedom for the consumer
d2= 5 meaning there is a gain in freedom for the consumer
Culminatively, there is a gain of 5 with the mixed model.


Now for the fully open hardware scenario:

choices/decisions:
1. free software on previously closed hdware.  d1
      d1=5 because the user gains support on previously closed hdware
2. run free software on open hdware,  d2 
      d2=5 because the user maintains support on open hdware

Culminatively, there is a total customer benefit of 10 in the fully open model, opposed to the previous mixed closed and open model where there was only a gain of 5.   We weren't even using negative numbers.  If we did, the results would be even more pro opening hardware.

[quote=slux]One of the things I do daily at my work is administering over a hundred GNU/Linux desktops. They often need new software installed. Using synaptic and manually selecting the packages, clicking install and closing the application is not a practical solution for this. A command line script iterating over every hostname and issuing apt-get commands over ssh is.

A smaller scale task that one might want to achieve on his personal system could be as simple as converting a set of images that are contained in albums inside a single directory (many subdirectories). I have wanted to do this (or maybe similar things to audio files) many times. Trying to achieve this thru a GUI is either a lot of manual work or involves finding a separate application for the sole task while it's very easy from the bash prompt.
[/quote]
Yeah, but now you're talking about scripting. I always had AppleScript for these things in OS 6-9. Could even make a 'droplet' on which I dropped the folder of images that should be resized for example. AppleScript also could do network operations. In other words, the command line should be a feature, not a requirement for a GUI system. Having something like it available in a GUI system is nice, but I doubt I ever gave the slightest idea of eliminating xTerm out of the GUI :).
What I'd like to see is a GUI what is excellent for beginners, but also for diehard command line junkies. Installation, configuration, organization, oversight(!!) etc. And of course you can write a GUI app that scans all computers in your network and let you select them to install apps on them, much easier as adding them all by hand to a script and it works if the configuration changes as well :). For a webserver I currently rely on and swear by the command line and of course the sh scripts, but that doesn't have to change. You set something like that up once and never touch it directly again.
